JOSEPH S. TOBIN, WHOSE ABLE ADDRESSES HAVE MADE HIM THE FAVORITE CANDIDATE.
STATEMENT GIVEN TO THE CALL IN WHICH THE CANDIDATE
SUPPLEMENTS PREVIOUS DECLARATIONS FROM THE PLATFORM
THE campaign is over. A. duty has been performed. We have denned the issues and presented them to the public. To the plan of campaign formulated at the outset we have consist
ently adhered. Our purpose lias been to address the citizens of Sin Francisco not in any partisan char-icter but as taxpayers and business men.
That this practical and reasonable method of reaching the mind of the impartial citizsn has been sif active cannot be denied. Day by day we have strengthened our position
until now we unquestionably hold the first place in the public mind.
Taking Into consideration the position
that v c occupied when this campaign was
initiated, our present position is a sure
indication of success. It could not be
Otherwise. To present the problem of mu
nicipal government to the public mind as
it has been educated and elevated by
study and experience in any other guise
than as a question purely of practical
business would be absurd.
The debates and discussions indent to
the creation of the present charter; the
turning over !n the minds of the public
of the principles of municipal government
at the lime when the charter was present
ed to the people for adoption; the contro
versies that have arisen by reason of the
change from the consolidation act to tlvi
charterâ all these things have had the ef
f< ot of impressing upon the public mind
ti.e principle that municipal government
i* a purely domestic affair.
That the charter has been a success
SAN FRANCISCO, MONDAY, NOVEMBER 4, 1901.
is unquestionable; that It promises to re
sult even more favorably for the welfare
of the city when it shall have become :i
more familiar instrument of municipal
government in ihe hands of the officers oC
the city is also unquestionable. But it
must be continued in operation; it must
continue to be handled by men nominated
by conventions in sympathy with the
spirit of the charter and by men whoso
political character indicates a spirit in
sympathy with the scope and spirit of tha
charter.
Issues Ara Identical.
We have ell e.ong contended that thes*
two issues are, as it were, identical. A
government by the charter, under the
charter and through the charter must hi
a government imploying practical busi
ness methods. We contend that all thosÂ»
who inaugurated and have thus far car
ried forward with success a movement for
clean municipal government in San Fran-
Cisco must support us now. We ltnow
that all those v/ho are interested in .t
proper businesslike administration of the
charter, who are interested in making Sa.t
Francisco wholesome and livable, must
support us, for to this end we have di
rected all cur efforts in the past, and to
this end we shall direct all our efforts in
the future.
We make this appeal to all such citi
zens, and we are convinced that they will
support us. It has been said of the great
Carlyle that he v/ent through life shed
ding the light of his intellect upon the
world in search of a man, but secretly de
lighted when he found a monkey. The
citizens of San Franci3co have for years
and years been in search of an honest,
clean municipal government. Should they
now repudiate that government they will
be placed in the cynical attitude toward
domestic affairs that Carlyle occupied to
ward the human race. But no, there are
no, such citizens. The audiences that we
have addressed in various parts of San
Frar.cisco, by their enthusiasm and atten
tion, have convinced us that all the citi
zens have that same goal in view.
Saved Society Millions.
We have saved the city of San Fran
cisco more than a million dollars. We
have reduced taxation, have reduced the
water rates, have reduced the gas rates,
have improved the Â«treets, have kept the
public buildings absolutely clean, have
beautified the parks throughout the city
and created new ones, and we have done
all this- without the taint of scandal or
'a charge of fraud.
All those who have come in contact with
the present Beard of Supervisors know
that in order to secure the passage cf a
bill or ordinance, the granting of a license
cr any other legislative act, it was . in
cumbent upon the petitioner to present t>
meritorious case. The condition of th

Executive Board of the Building Trades
Council Finds Union Labor Candidate
for Mayor Guilty of Charges as Made
rHE executive board of the Building Trades Council last
night officially declared Eugene E. Schmitz, the Union Labor
candidate for Mayor, to be guilty of the charges of ''scab
bing" preferred against him by three members of the machinists'
craft. A thorough investigation 9 of the charges that Schmitz had
secretly dealt with non-union iron concerns was made zvith the result
that findings zvere returned to the council declaring the charges fully
proved. The standard bearer of the Union Labor party zvas de
nounced as being a traitor to the cause of unionism in San Fran
cisco, increasing by his acts the misery suffered by the families -of
striking machinists. _____
THE executive board of the Build- '
ing Trades Council last night
officially declared Eugene E.
Bchmitz, candidate for Mayor on
the Union Labor ticket, to be un
worthy of the support of the
workingmen of San Francisco.
The meeting of the board was a stormy
one and no conclusion was reached until
midnight. The testimony of Messrs.
Reardon, Twomey and Lane, who signed
the charges against Schmitz, was heard
at length, and the verdict of guilty was
reached unanimously.
When isie meeting was called to order
by Acting Chairman Harry Costen. the
subject matter was at once gone into, and
City Hall itself presents an object lesson.
By the lawyers and litigants who were
compelled to visit it from day to day it
was formerly looked "upon as a disgrace
,to the city.
It is now thoroughly cleaned and reno
vated and In cleanliness and appearance
is the equal cf any large building in t >-.vn.
This in Itself is a small matter, but it m
clcates the practical utility of the charter.
Ambitious to Serve City.

campaign argument?. If elected Mayor 1
shall go into the office free and untram
meled, free from corporation control and
free from obligations to any boss. My
sole ambition is to serve the city with all
[ the ability at my command, faithfully
i and zealously. Progresr, and economy
| shall be my watchwords. San Franci.sc )
|is now at the parting of the ways. Be

SCHMITZ IS FOUND GUILTY ON CHARGES OF DEALING WITH NON-UNION SHOPS
AND IS DENOUNCED BY THE BUILDING TRADES COMMITTEE AS TRAITOR TO LABOR
REARDON SAYS
SCHMITZ HAS
PLAYED FALSE
â¢ Â«âÂ« â
Union Man Faces Labor
Candidate's Partisans
and Repeats Charges
Controversy Comes Near
to Ending in Tragedy
on the Public Streets
THE meeting of the executive board
of thÂ« Building Trades Council at
7 City Hall Square, held for
the purpose of investigating
the charges against Eugene E.
Schmitz, candidate for Mayor on
the Union Labor ticket, came very
near resulting la a tragedy yesterday ait
ernoon.
The lie Vas passed, personalities were
indulged in by a group of Schmitz sympa
thizers who had evidently gathered to In
timidate the witnesses who were to appear
before the board, and a pistol was drawn.
But for the prompt interference of by
standers, the sequel to the filing of the
charges against Schmitz might have been
murder.
The trouble was precipitated by Joseph
Millmore. chairman of the late labor con
vention, Thomas McConnell, a shipwright
and a teamster named Eckles. These men,
surrounded by a number of followers, all
adherents of Schmitz and other labor can
didates, were grouped in front of the Car
pet Mechanics' headquarters on City Hall
Square when T. A. Reardon and Ed Two
mey. the machinists who tiled the charges
against Schmitz. approached. They were
to give their testimony before the board
ana this fact was made the pretext by
the Sctimitz men named to call them to
account for tailing the course they did.
"You're a pair of -scaos,' " said Mill
more to Rearaon and Twomey, as he
nourished a cane excitedly.
"1 can prove that," added McConnell an
grily.
Reardon answered that he was not there
to answer unofficial charges. If the per
sons addressed were not satisfied with mm
they might report the fact to the Council.
Beardon Draws a Revolver.
The discussion became general. The
Scnmitz men appeared to be greatly exer
cised at what they termed the rascality
of the three men who hied the charges
against Schmitz, and they upbraided them
in forcible language for making charges
Â¦which, they asserted, it was impossible lor
any one to substantiate. Reardon indig
nantly denied that he had made false
charges.
"1 have told the truth!" shouted Rear
don, with his hand at his hip pocket. "'I
say Schmitz did do 'scab' work during the
strike, and your statement that he was
given permission ro do the work by the
Iron Trades Council is false. McCabe and
Wieler are the whole works as far as the
Council is concerned, and their statement
in answer to the charges that permission
was given Schmitz to cio scab work is un
true. 1 defy them to produce the minutes
of the meeting at which this permission
was granted, is recorded. They don't dare
to show the minutes, because the books
would prove both McCabe and Wissler to
be liars. My reputation for truth is as
good as theirs, and 1 stand on my charge
that Schmitz is a 'scab* and unlit to be
Mayor. He may have pulled the wool
over your eyes, but he can't do it in my
case."
Millmore made some angry retort
whereupon Reardon sprang back with the
remark that if any one eot behind him he
would shoot. Quick as a flash he drew a
revolver and held it before him on a line
with Millmore's heart. Millmore remained
motonless as a statue, but said nothing to
further Infuriate the angry Reardon.
President McCarthy, of the Building
Trades Council, stepped between the par
ties at this juncture, and Reardon was
induced to pocket his weapon. He an
nounced his intention to shoot the first
man that offered to molest him during the
proceedings which were pending.
Will Swear Out Warrants.
Twomey announced also that he would
not permit any one to molest him, where
upon Eckles made some insulting remark
which Twomey resented with a blow on
Eckles' neck. Bystanders separated the
belligerents, and McCarthy and some
friends hustled Reardon and Twomey Into
a side room of the headquarters, while the
Sehmitz men on the outside consulted as
to the course to pursue. After a few mo
ments Millmore and Eckles announced
their intention of swearing out a warrant
for the arrest of Reardon and Twomey.
the first for displaying a revolver in a
threatening fashion, and the other for his
assault upon Eckles. The party adjourned
to the City Hall police station and formal
ly lodged their complaints, but the war
rant was not Issued. The Schmitz adher
ents then started down town in search of
a Police Judge who might Issue the de
sired warrant for the arrest of Reardon
and Twomey.
The incident created considerable excite
ment, and an immense crowd gathered in
the square in anticipation of further
trouble. On the adjournment of the ex
ecutive board the crowd slowly dispersed

FORMAL FINDINGS AGAINST CANDIDATE SCHMITZ
ARE REPORTED BY THE UNIONIST COMMITTEE
THE executive board of the Building Trades Council, having been directed by the council to investigate the charges made against Eugene E. Schmitz, ln
ion Labor party candidate for the office of Mayor, after a thorough and careful consideration of the entire subject matter, presents without fear or favor the
following preamble and findings:
PREAMBLE. â¢ %
The Building Trades Council is a central body which represents in its entirety all labor engaged in the buikling industry.
The Building Trades Council has at all times endeavored to promote the best interests of the city, State and nation. No power or influence of whatso
ever nature behind any political candidate will induce the Council to support an unworthy person, be he a union man or not.
In judging the fitness for office the Council considers the trustworthiness, competency and attitude toward labor of political candidates, regardless of
affiliation or partisanship, opposing only those who by reason of their acts are unworthy of labor's support.
FiisriDiisra-s.
No. 1. We find upon oral and documentary evidence that Eugene E. Schmitz has since June 3 (date of molders' walk-out) purchased the product of non
union molders at Liberty Iron Works, Enterprise Foundry and Beale-street Foundry.
No. 2. That he has continued to do so until Wednesday, October 30, 1901, at which time a cylinder for the Economist Gas Engine Company was dis
covered at the Beale-street Foundry (non-union), and upon the facts becoming known by the president of the Iron Trades Council was immediately removed.
No. 3. That Eugene E. Schmitz between June 18 and August 20, 1901, built for the Risdon Iron Works two ammonia compressors with engines at
tached; that said Risdon Iron Works was then and is now non-union.
No. 4. That Eugene E. Schmitz upon being challenged to refute these charges has failed to do so, but on the contrary, to excuse his treachery; has
brought forth Messrs. McCabe and Wisler, who, according to their statement in the Examiner of to-day, admit Schmitz's guilt in the premises.
No. 5. That McCabe and Wisler by this statement admit their treachery to all union men now on strike, and reveal a plot to boom Mr. Schmitz's can
didacy at the cost of every 'aboring man in the iron trades.
No. 6. That to excuse Mr. Schmitz's conduct Messrs. McCabe and Wisler admit having consented to and sanctioned dealings with non-union concerns,
which have resulted in the prolongation of the strike and serious damage to the iron trades cause.
No. 7. That McCabe and Wisler before this, the executive board of the Building Trades Council, refused to honestly answer questions seeking thorough
information on the charges made, and whatever answers they made were weak and evasive. Both gentlemen resorted to subterfuge in o^r to shield Schmitz.
In view of the foregoing we are convinced that Eugene E. Schmitz has violated every principle of unionism; that prior to and during his candidacy he
has done immeasurable harm to the cause of the iron trades; that Messrs. McCabe and Wisler, to further Schmitz's candidacy, have dishonestly assumed his
guilt, hoping to prevent an upheaval against Schmitz; that it is a regrettable fact that the loyal trades unionists in the iron trades are to-day suffering for the
necessaries of life as a result of the double dealing of William McCabei R. I. Wisler and Eugene E. Schmitz. HARRY COSTEN, Chairman Pro Tern.

fore us lie two roads. One leads back
to the old condition of business stagna
tion, realty depression, corrupt City Hall
rings, wasteful public expenditures and
oppressive public burdens.
The other is the broad highway to the
high and commanding destiny, which
opens its glorious vistas before this city,
so favored as it is by its surpassing nat
ural advantages. I love my native city
and it would be a sufficient reward for
all my labors in her behalf, however
humble or however weak, if I could feel
that they contributed in any degree to the
welfare of her citizens or her permanent
improvements. I would enter upon the
office, not as a partisan, but as a servant
of the whole people, determined to do my
duty to the whole people, as God gives
me light to see it.

storm of applause from the workingmen
who composed his audience.

profits through his position as attorney*

CHINESE IN BATTLE
ON VANCOTJVEB ISLAND
Tongs Begin a War and the Authori
ties Are Fearful of Wholesale
Assassination.
VANCOUVER, B. C, Nov. 3.â Half a
dozen Chinese residents of Cumberland,
Vancouver Island, are in jail as the re
sult of a bitter fight between different
tongs of Chinese. The Celestials first be
gan to fight over the arrest of the chief
of one of the factions and now the trou
ble has become so fierce that the .eading
Chinese on both sides have been arrested.
The first fight on Friday night was a
riot in which a hundred Chinese took
part. Several shots were exchanged and
one man was wounded, though not se
riously. There is talk of the assassina
tion of the leaders and the authorities
are keeping- a careful watch to prevent
more trouble.
ENRAGED GREEKS TIE MEN TO THE RAILS
IN EFFORT TO COMMIT FRIGHTFUL CRIME
Engineer and a Section Boss at a Nevada Town Narrowly Escape Being
Murdered Under the Wheels of a Train at Night.
CARSON, Nev., Nov. 3.â Frank Krause, a locomotive engineer, and John Shea, a section boss, had thirty
minutes of terrible suspense in anticipation of a horrible fate at Beowawe, Nev., on the line of the
Southern Pacific Railroad, last Friday evening. Both were tied across a railroad track by a mob of infuriated
Greeks and were doomed to be crushed by a train which was soon to pass. The earnest pleadings of their
friends accomplished their release a few minutes before the headlight of the engine that would have ended
their lives flashed upon the rails.
This atrocious attempt at murder grew out of a saloon brawl in which Krause and Shea were concerned.
Both men engaged in an argument, during which Krause drew his revolver and fired at Shea. The bullet missed
its mark and struck a Greek laborer in the shoulder, passing through his body. This accident enraged the
Greeks and about sixty of them, employed as section men, seized the participants and hurried them down the
railroad track to a point about half a mile from the town. A few friends of the prisoners followed, but did
not take the situation seriously, as they little guessed the intentions of the captors.
When the men were tied to the rails those who followed attempted to release them, but were beaten back
by the Greeks, who declared that both captives should be cut to pieces by the first train that passed. Several
times the friends of the men made an effort to rescue them, but their endeavors were, futile. At first it was
hoped the Greeks only intended to frighten the men and no messenger was sent back to the town for assistance.
When the pure cold-bloodedness of the preparations was better realized by the friends of the men, the train that
was to complete the fiendish work was due in a few minutes. Several of the party that had followed the Greeks
tried to escape from the crowd for the purpose of flagging the train, but were prevented from doing so. Mean
while the intended victims were piteously pleading for their lives. At a most fortunate moment the station
agent came to the scene, and at "his earnest- request, which was subsequently supplemented by threats, the men
were given their liberty. They had scarcely risen from the track when the train thundered by.
After Krause and Shea reached Beowawe they lost no time in packing their belongings and leaving the
vicinity.

WOULD OPEN ISLANDS
TO ASIATIC COOLIES
Acting Governor of Hawaii Appeals
for the Admission of Chinese
Labor.
WASHINGTON. Nov. 3.â Authority for
the conversion of all Hawaiian silver
coins into corresponding coins of the
United States, and for the immigration of
a limited number of Chinese laborers, con
ditioned upon their engaging in agricultu
ral pursuits only during their residence in
the Territory and their return to their
own country upon ceasing to be farmers,
are the recommendations of H. E. Cooper,
acting Governor of Hawaii, in hla annual
report.
other recommendations are for the
maintenance of an expert forester on the
islands to investigate and report on forest
conditions and forestry conservation and
extension: the detail of a special agent
of the Fish Commission at Honolulu and
the adoption of restrictive measures to
prevent wanton destruction of the food
tishes of the Territory; the granting of li
censes to divert water from lands where
it is of no avail to arid sections that own
ers are adapting to agricultural pursuits;
the erection of a Federal building in Hon
olulu for the accommodation of the Fed
eral Court, Postofflce, Custom-house and
Federal officials; the retention from the
customs receipts of a sufficient sum to pay
judgments of the Commission of Fire
Claims; the gathering of statistics to
show the shipments between the mainland
and the islands, and the appointment of
a Deputy Secretary to act during the dis
ability of the Secretary of T .he Treasury
tor Territorial purposes only.
The report snows that, though there
has been an increase on the part of the
Hawaiians, the total of those of Hawaiian
descent shows a decrease, the most rapid
in the commercial centers. The prosperity
of the islands, according to the actine;
Governor, depends upon the successful and
economical production of sugar
'The employment of Chinese and Japa
nese by the planters," he says, "never
has interfered and never will interfere
with either American skilled or unskilled
labor, but if an ample supply of desirable
labor can be obtained it means millions of
dollars spent for machinery, buildings
tools, steel and iron and other supplies of
American make."

LEAVE PRISON
WITH AN ENGINE
Sensational Escape of
Two Men From
Yuma's Jail.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
YUMA, Ariz., Nov. 3.â During the noon
hour to-day Thomas Hart, under sentence
for murder of Under Sheriff Devane at
this place last January, and a Mexican
named Leibas, in Jail for robbery, over
powered the guard of the Jail as the door
was opened to pass in their dinner.
Locking the guard in the cell and taking
two rifles and a revolver from the Sher
iff's office, the two men made their escape
into the railroad yards, where a switch
engine was on the turntable. Leibas for
merly worked in the roundhouse and was
able to manage an engine.
After compelling the engineer and fire
man to get off, he took charge of the en
gine, while Hart threw the switch neces
sary to put them on the main line and
got aboard. The engine was run out
about twelve miles from town and aban
doned.
Another engine with a car containing a
posse quickly followed in pursuit of the
escaped prisoners. The latest report is
that the men were encountered near Fort
una. a small mining camp, and a desper
ate battle ensued, resulting in the killing
of Hart and capture of Leibas.

They were married before coming to
Phoenix, but the first weding in England
two years ago lacked civil confirmation.
The bride is heir to a vast English es
tate and married Sloan against the
wishes of her relatives. Lawyers and de
tectives have sought vainly to bring
about a separation, and under fear of
being arrested by Los Angeles attorneys
employed by the estate, the pair fled to
Phoenix and caused the civil ceremony
to be quietly performed.
Sloan, with his wife, appeared on the
stage in the East, In Denver and on the
Pacific Coast under the name of Richard
Berford.

BOERS DISPLAY
GREAT BRAVERY
Charge the British Lines
Repeatedly and Lose
Heavily.
Botha Personally Directs the
Attack Upon Colonel Ben
son's Command.
PRETORIA. Nov. 3.â Further details
have been received regarding the attack
by the Boers under the command of Gen
eral Botha last week on Colonel Benson's
command near Brakenlaagte, Eastern
Transvaal. It appears that General Bo
tha, who had been joined by another 1 big
commando, aggregating 1000 men, attack
ed Colonel Benson's rear guard on October
30 on the march and captured two guns,
but was unable to keep them.
Colonel Besnon fell mortally wounded
early in the fight. Major Wools-Sampson
took command, collected the convoy and
took up a position for defense about 100
yards from entrenchments prepared by
the Boers. The captured guns were so
situated that neither side could touch
them. â¢
The Boers made desperate /efforts to
overwhelm the whole British force, charg
ing repeatedly right up to the British lines
and being driven back each time with
heavy loss. The defense was stubbornly
and successfully maintained through the
whole of the following day and the next
night, until Colonel Barter, who had
marched all night from Bushaman's kop
je, brought relief on the morning of No
vember 1.
The Boers then retired. Their losses are
estimated at between 300 and 400. Colonel
Benson did not long survive.
Not only did General Botha direct the
attack, as already cabled, but he person
ally shared in the fighting.

j cally nothing iurther about the disaster
to Colonel Benson's column. It appears
that the first attack was made in a blind
ing rainstorm.

Pro-Boers Attacked by Mob.
A riotous scene occurred yesterday in
Peckham, a suburb of London. A small
gathering of the local branch of the
Democratic League undertook to hold a
meeting to protest against the war in
South Africa. The manifestatloners start
ed in procession for the place of meeting,
but were hooted, buffeted and dispersed.
A crowd estimated at 20,000 gathered to
oppose them. There were many ugly
rushes and the police were barely able to
protect the struggling pro-Boers from the
fury of the populace. After much fight
ing, during which a mar* was stabbed, tlu
pro-Boers were removed under police pro
tection. The victorious crowd then held
a jingo meeting and sang "Rule Britan
nia."
Dispatches from Warsaw say the news
papers there assert that a contract has
been entered into for the supply of 20,00'J
. horses for the British cavalry in South
Africa.
Queen Alexandra, following the exam
ple of the late Queen Victoria, is sendinar
Christmas gifts to the troops in South
Africa. For this purpose she has ordered
thousands of brier pipes, each silver
mounted and bearing the stamp of the
crown and her Majesty's monogram.
KING EDWARD SPEAKS
OF WAR IN SOUTH ATBICA
Firmly Hopes and. Prays for the Re
establishment of Peace and
Prosperity.
LONDON, Nov. 4.â At the dinner on
board the royal yacht Victoria and Albert
last Friday evening King Edward, toast
ing the Duke and Duchess of Cornwall
and York, gave a sketch of their tour.
Referring to their arrival at Cape Town
his Majesty said: "There, unfortunately,
the war is prolonged, but we firmly pray
for the re-establishment of peace and
prosperity."
In conclusion the King said: "In all the
colonies thus visited they fulfilled their
mission, expressing the gratitude of the
mother country for the aid generously ao
ibrded her in the hour of need, and they
were everywhere received with a cor
diality of loyal enthusiasm which could
not have been surpassed. The accounts
of these receptions have touched me
deeply and I trust the practical results
will be to draw closer the strong ties of
mutual affection which bind together the
old motherland and her numerousf*thriv
ing offsprings."
The Duke of Cornwall in a felicitous re
sponse testified to the "intense and en
thusiastic loyalty shown by the people
everywhere to the King and the throne,
and also to "the deep love of the mother
country, which was everywhere spoken of
as 'home.' "

Captain Handy had been a pensioner in

He was last May tried before Judge Grir
fin in the Superior Court and convicted of
having been the accomplice of Dr. E. F.
Adams in the alleged swindling of Dr.
J Eugene Jordan of this city to the tune
of $10,000 in the sale of the ancient steam
ship Professor Morse, in Oakland harbor,
in the spring of 1898.
Famous Criminal Case.
The case has become famous In the
criminal annals of King County. Dr.
Adams, whose home ia at Chicago, was
first tried. The jury in his case dis
agreed. Pending a second trial he was
released on heavy bonds and is now said
to be in Texas. Captain Handy was less
fortunate and subsequently to hi 9 arrest
was unable to obtain bonds, and was
kept a prisoner in the County Jail. His
bail was fixed at $5000 after he was tried
last spring, found guilty and sentenced.
Captain Handy's home is in San Fran
cisco He came to Seattle several yÂ»ars
ago He is well known all along the coast.
He is 50 years of age, 5 feet 10 inches tall,
rather heavily built, weighs about 200
pounds and wears a Van Dyke beard
closely cropped. His right arm is crippled.
During his long residence In the county
jail Captain Handy established for him
self the reputation of being a model pris
oner and was as a consequence allowed
many liberties not accorded ordinary in
mates of the prison. To the fact that the
rules were relaxed In his case is largely
due his present liberty.

news Is not surprising, as the instructions

zations. Their intent must be evil. No matter

BOTH CLAIM NEW JERSEY.
Republicans and Democrats Close a
Stubborn Campaign.
NEW YORK, Nov. 3.â The gubernato
rial campaign in New Jersey was brought
to a close last night, vith the leaders oÂ£
both parties claiming victory for their re
spective candidates. It was one of the
most stubborn campaigns ever fought la
the State, the Republicans leaving no
stone unturned to hold the State machin
ery, which they have controlled for nearly
a decade, while the Democrats struggled
to take it from them. Both sides are to
night claiming victory.
The Republicans base their hopes on
two thingsâ first, the large majority which
must be wiped out by the opposition, and,
second, that they believe the voters of the
State, pleased at the prosperous condition
of affairs in the nation, will return a Re
publican Governor as an evidence of their
indorsement of the national administra
tion and as an assurance to the present
head of the administration that they have
confidence in him. It was principally on
the latter issue that the party made its
fight.
The Democrats claim victory on the
personal popularity of their candidate for
Governor, James M. Seymoyr. He is very
popular with the working classes and his
strength is acknowledged by his oppo
nents. He has thrice been elected Mayor
of Newark by large majorities.
Both State committees have compiled
tablee made up from reports from various
counties of the State. The Republicans
by their figures show that Franklin Mur
phy, their candidate for Governor, who i 3
also chairman of the committee, will be
elected by about 1000 majority. The tables
compiled by William E. Gourley, chair
man of the Democratic State Committee,
shows that Mayor Seymour will have
about 8000 majority.

ALL ARE MADE COMMISSARIES.
Government Employes in Philippines
May Deal in Supplies.
WASHINGTON. Nov. 3.â The Civil Ser
vice Commission has received word that
under an act jusl passed by the Philip
pine Commission all employes of the in
sular and provincial governments sta
tioned outside of the city of Manila will
receive commissary privileges. Commis
sary supplies will be sold them at an in
crease of 20 per cent of the actual cost, to
defray the transportation and necessary
expenses. The headquarters and disburs
ing stores will be located at Manila.
About t>ooo employes are benefited. This
action follows the general complaint of
school teachers sent to the Philippines
that Government assurances as to fur
nishing of supplies were not executed.

LIGHT VOTE IN NEBRASKA.
Storm Responsible for Big Decrease in
the Registration.
LINCOLN. x\eb., Nov. 3.â Yesterday's
storm played havoc with the registration
,in this city, which shows a falling off of
more than 2000 compared with a year ago.
The same report comes from other large
towns in the State.
This is not encouraging to the Republi
cans, but as the same apathy is shown
in the city they assert they see no cause
for alarm. The only rally of importance
to-morrow will be at South Omaha, where
W. J. Bryan will make a number oÂ£
speeches at night.
At the two State headquarters the re
spective chairman said to-night they saw
no reason to change their estimates pre
viously made. Both committees predict
a light vote.

COST OF NAVAL STATION.
! Proposed Philippine Island Plant Will
Require Many Millions.
WASHINGTON. Nov. 3.â The majority
report of the naval board, headed by Rear
Admiral Taylor, which has prepared plans
for the proposed new naval station at
Olongapo, P. 1., shows that the station
may cost this Government $19,359,500, ex
clusive of defensive works, signal stt
tlons, water supply, coaling station, mag
azines, hospital, encampment grounds and
a native village under the control of the
naval authorities, which it is proposed to
provide for the workmen who will be em
ployed regularly at the Olongapo dock
yards.
The board recommends that, in addition
to the four prominent points selected for
the defensive works by Admiral Remey's
boardâ Sllanguln Island, at the entrance
of Port Silanguin; the point on the west
side of the entrance to Subig Bay, Grande
Island at the midentraitce and Point Ilian
on the east side of Subig Bay entranceâ
another fort be located to protect the
landing in Calaguaquin Bay, on the west
coast of ZambeleÃ province, and that the
mountain paths leading to the naval res
ervation on the north and east be forti
fied. The board's estimate for equipping
the station for efficient work in repairing
and outfitting vessels, pending general de
velopment, is $12,903,500. . "
The report concludes with a statement
that much of the material for the sta
tion must come from the United States
though a good part of it can be obtained
at Hongkong. . â¢

IRISH PEOPLE
AGAIN UNITED
John E. Redmond Tells
of Their Struggle for
Liberty.
Reception Given in New York
to Nationalist Leader of
. Parliament \
â¢Â¦"â¢â¢ â¢ "Â¦ ./ ~ , ..Â¦.Â¦>;-:Â¦ - .:- Â¦ -Â¦ Â¦ â¢-Â¦
NEW YORK, Nov. 3.â lrish-Americans
to the number of 3000 or 4000 from this city
and vicinity gathered to-night at a recep
tion given at Carnegie Hall in \ honor, of
John E. Redmond, M. P., leader of the
Irish Nationalist. party in the British Par
liament. The reception was given under
the auspices of the Amalgamated Irish
Societies in 1 Nfw York, the Irish National
Club and the Irish National League. The
boxes of the upper and lower tier were
occupied by the delegates of the various
Irish societies in this ;-'-.V>
. Redmond was accompanied by Patrick
McHugh and Thomas O'Donnell, his fel
low-delegates from the National Â¦ League.
William McAdoo, â¢ former assistant Secre
tary of the Navy, was chairman of the
evening and "introduced Redmond, who
said in part: Â¦Â¦'/:Â¦ Â¦
When I. last spoke her* Ireland wag torn by
dissensions.. She was divided, distracted and
weak. The great leader who had | banded - the
I: ish race- together all over tie world and had
brought the pause of Ireland to the very thresh
old of victory had -disappeared., the great move
ment which his genius had created had, been
wrecked, the hopes of the Â¦ Irish race had been
dashed to the ground and the enemies of- Ire
land were exultant, and. triumphant. To-night,
thank God, the situation is changed. It is .true,
unfortunately, that the grave cannot give up
its dead; it is true that there is no great politi
cal genius- to-day presiding over, the councils of
the Irish nation, but the Irish people themselves
have come together again. . : Â¦ â¢ "' â¢
â¢ My object in coming here 1?, in the first place,
to proclaim the unity of the Irish people at
home; secondly, to explain the policy which
these people at home have unanimously adopt
ed, and. finally, to say to the Irish. race In
America that if they approve of that policy
they ought to support It, but that whether
they support it or â whether the Irish peo
ple at home are left in- this "crisis of : their
country's history to carry on this struggle
against England without the aid of their breth
ren" throughout the world or not, we at home
are disciplined enough and -strong enough and
earnest enough to carry on this movement our
selves and at any cost we shall do so. a
Redmond said. that the policy, of the
United Irish League movement was to ad
vance the national cause of Irishmen by
making the government of Ireland by
England, in every department, both in
Parliament and out of Parliament, diffi
cult and dangerous," and finally impossible.
He paid it whs proposed to accomplish this
by means of the United' Irish League or
ganization, the lineal successor of the
Land League.
; AlcHugh and O'Donnell followed Red
mond in speaking and ! resolutions were
adopted Indorsing the policy of the league
and pledging the - support of the meeting
to its policy. \. . . Â¦
MANY SAFEGUARDS THROWN
AROUND DOWAGER EMPRESS
Chinese Officials Do Not Deny That an
Attempt Was Made to Assas
sinate Her.
PEKING, Nov. 3.â The Chinese officials
do not deny that an attempt was made to
assassinate the Dowager Empress shortly
before the court's arrival at Honan a few
days ago, but say they have received no
authentic official information. This state
ment Is regarded as confirmatory of the
report. The court is now in the region
of the allied villages society, the fear of
which caused the change of the imperial
route lying over the southern part of the
province of Chili. The effect of the at
tempt to murder her Majesty will be to
increase the safeguards surrounding 1 her.
It is reported that between 20.000 and 30,000
troops are attending the imperial party.
Work of Industrial Commission Over.
WASHINGTON, Nov. 3.â The Industrial
Commission will cease to exist on De
cember 15 by operation of the law. The
members of the commission are now at
home, mostly to vote in the coming State
elections. A full meeting of the commis
sion will be held next Wednesday and
the commission probably will remain in
session most of the month going over its
final report. This will be embodied In one
volume and will be submitted to Con
gress about the time the official exist
ence of the commission expires.

Bearing Admiral's Body Home.
NEW YORK, Nov. 3.-The Spanish
steamship Montserrat, on her way home
from Havana, came into port to-day with
her flags at half mast. In a mortuary
apartment aboard the liner was the body
of Admiral Ville, who lost his life during
the encounter with the American ships at
Santiago. The body is on the way to
Spain. Before the Montserrat leaves the
Spanish Consul and many of the Spanish
residents of this city will attend memo
rial services aboard the steamer.
Friends Fear He Was Killed.
GRASS VALLEY, Nov. 3.â For several
weeks Eugene Gray has been missing
from Nevada City. He was employed in
a livery stable there, and after feeding
the horses one morning he suddenly dis
appeared. He left all his possessions be
hind. He had an excellent reputation,
and his friends fear foul play, though it
is not known that he had any amount of
money with him.
Oliver Stevens of Boston has been the
County District Attorney for twenty-sev
en consecutive years. He is a Democrat,
but has been twice re-elected by the Re
publicans.

SINKS ALONGSIDE SHIP CALIFORNIA
Collides With the Schooner Anchored Over the Wreck of
the May Flint â James Coulton, the Fireman, Is Missing,
but Captain Richardson Says He Was Not Drowned
THERE was a narrow escape from
a terrible disaster on the bay Sat
urday nig-ht. As it is. the stern
wheel steamboat Phoenix Is at
the bottom of the bay and one of
her crew is missing.
The Phoenix came here from Eureka
p^rae weeks ago and was added to the
Piper-Aden, Goodall Company's fleet. She
has been towing rock barges to and from
Sherman Island for the contractor that is
filing in China Basin for the Santa Fe.
Sarurday night she started out from Mis-
Bajr with a barjro in tow, and Cap
tain Richardson thought he would have
no trouble in clearing all obstructions. In
stead, however., when the scene of the
May Flint wreck was reached the crew
Â«=tM- that the wrecking barge anchored
over the danger spot could not be cleared.
Ar. attempt was made to cut the Phoe
tow adrift, but it failed and the
steamboat went broadside onto the wreck- ;
ins schooner. She began to fill and James
Coulton, the fireman, is said to have :
jumped from her to the barge. He has
-en seen nor heard from since, and
ms ny of his friends were around the front
locking for him yesterday.
-oon as the Phoenix struck she be- ;
ga i to leak like a sieve, and in a few ;
minutes the inrushing water had drowned
ou" the fires under the boilers. Captain
Riohardson at once headed the vessel for
Htrrison-street wharf and notified the !
engineer to give her all steam. She- rush
ed along with everything wide open, and j
th<>re was just en .ugh steam left in her !
boilers to carry her up alongside the ship |
California. She struck the latter gently,
stsyed afloat long enough to give the crew
time to clamber aboard the ship and then
â¢went down. The chances are that she
wi 1 be raised to-day and taken to the Po
trero for repairs.
Captain Richardson said:
The accident was dup to the unusually strong
tiflf. WÂ« were carried down on the wrecking
schooner anchored over the remains of the May
Flint in spite of all that tteam could do. 1
<Jor"t think Coulton went down with the steam
er, as come one jumped aboard the barge I was
towing as soon ac we struck. The lookout yelled j
to me that one of my men was being left behind,
but I was too anxious to get alongside a wharf
to wait and pick him -up. The Phoenix is
pretty badly etove and will be laid up for sev
eral months undergoing repairs.

Water Front Notes.
Four vessels of the Charles Nelson Com
pany's fleet had a race from Eureka
â¢which ended yesterday. They were all
lumber laden, and while two of them
made almost steamboat time, the other
two were away behind. The Ottillie Fjord
came down In forty-eight hours, while
the Laura Pike was only five hours be
hind her. The Guide and Mary Buhne
both took seventy-two hours to come
down. All four schooners will be sent
bÂ£.ck to Humboldt Bay and will have a
chance to see what they can do when in
ballast.
The German steamship Sesostrls pre
sented a peculiar spectacle yesterday. She
was discharging coal from one hatch and
receiving It in another. She was coaled
at. South America and the fuel Is no good.
It has, therefore, been taken out and
hard coal purchased here put in its place.

America for Americans.
Benjamin Fay Mills delivered a lecture
last night at Metropolitan Temple on
"America for Americans; or, What Shall
We Do With Our Foreigners?" Previous
to the lecture A. O. Eckman gave an or
gan recital and Mrs. Charles Poulter de
lighted the large audience with a vocal
selection, following which the congrega
tion joined in singing Kipling's famous
hymn, the "Recessional." The entertain
ment was concluded by the singing uf
"The Star-Spangled Banner" by Mrs.
Poulter and the singing of "America" by
the congregation.

cember 19, reaching Mexico In time for
the holiday celebrations. Round trip, San

conducted. Dining-car, Pullmar. sleepars.

Shot Fired on Third Street.
A shot was fired in front of the Sunrise
saloon, 245 Third street, shortly before 3
o'clock yesterday morning. Policemen
Moriarty and Clay heard the shot and
made an investigation, but could find no
trace of the shooter. Lew Calderallo, pro
prietor of the .saloon, told them there was
a disturbance cutside the saloon and he
seized a club and made for the door. Just
as he reached the door some one fired the
shot, but nobody was hurt.

and Republicans have broken away from
the heads of their tickets and are going
to vote for Dr. C. C. O'Donnell for Mayo-

column. â¢
LONDON MARKET
SHOWS NO LIFE
Rise in Bank of England
Rates Fails to Im
prove Trade.
Berlin Boerse Remains in a
Sluggish and Depressed
Condition.
LONDON, Nev. 3.â Conditions on the
Stock Exchange last week may be fairly
described as moribund. Money was the
dormant factor. The long predicted rise
in the Bank of England rate came just
when many had ceased to expect it, but
it had little effect in the sluggish state
of trading. Most of the professionals who
had no business to -transact made the
! homecoming of the Duke and Duchess of
Cornwall and York a good excuse for pre
tending not to do business.
Consols continued to fall with the force
of habit. Any recurrence of the recent
! regrettable incidents in South Africa
would be likely to bring them to 91 or
lower. It is interesting to consider upon
what terms the government will be able
to place the next loan, which is surely
due before long. One expert has sug
gested 88 for Â£20,000,000, but the issue,
when it comes, will certainly be for a
much larger amount.
In the meanwhile experts are figuring
whether the rise in the bank rate will
check the now of British gold to Paris.
The general impression is that it will,
thus torcing New \'ork to liquidate its >
own indebtedness to Paris, which is be
lieved to be enormous by shipping Amer
ican gold. Some dissent from this view, I
however, maintaining that Wall street is
concealing several trump cards which it
will play when the game warrants. Chief
among these is the still unsolved North
ern Pacific puzzle. Lntil an authorita
tive announcement is made no one here
will be able to estimate how America's
continental borrowings compare with her
immense trade balance.
Thus speculation without the main
facts, though interesting, is useless.
BERLIN, Nov. 2.â The Boerse last
week showed the same pessimistic phy
siognomy displayed during several weeks
previous. "It is nearly a lifeless body,"
said the Vossiache Zeitung, "and no
longer able to respond to outside influ
ences."
Business has grown so stagnant that
many first class shares could not be
quoted at the lirst make up list on some
days. It is significant that an effort is
1 being made to close the boerse at 2
j p. m. daily, the usual hour of Saturday
I closing.
News from the various industries con
tinues unfavorable. A number of meet
ings of stockholders of coal and iron
| companies were held during the week
i and the opinion was expressed that the
depression had not reached low water
mark. In spite of the discouraging pros
pects, however, quotations were fairly
well maintained, speculators asserting
that the present quotations sufficiently
discount the situation. The only depart
ment which showed life was American
rails, Northern Pacific shares leading.
j Ocean transportation shares weakened I
I upon thje rumor of a war of rates and of
j a continued decline in the spring busi
i ness. It is asserted that the harbor of
Hamburg is full of idle vessels. W hile j
the week brought no news of Industrial |
disaster, the unhealthy condition of the '
; companies is still reflected by the dally
I publication in the Reichsanzeiger of the
| names of directors who have resigned
j from various companies.
The monthly settlement has passed !
( very easily, money being abundant and !
cheap. The advance in the rate of the i
Bank of England produced scarcely any
effect here, but the Frankfurter Zeitung
says that Germany sooner or later must
reconcile herself to r igher rates.
A complete cessation of gold imports
from London has occurred.
EUROPEAN SUGAR
ON THE DECLINE
Marked Falling Off in the
Imports to United
States.
WASHINGTON, Nov. B.â "The obvious !
and sustained friendliness of the United
States Government to Cuba, the impend
ing Increase of su^ar production in Porto
Rico, Hawaii arid the Philippines, added
to the steady and rapid development of
the beet-sugar industry throughout a
large area of the United States, all point
to an inevitable and definite decline in.
European sugar exports to our country."
This statement appears In an interesting
report from Consul General Mason at
Berlin to the State Department, dated
October 4, showing the damage which the
sugar production of Cuba in particular is !
dealing to the sugar exporters of Europe. I
"It is generally recognized," says Ma
son, "that the beet-sugar industry of Cen
tral Europe is approaching a critical junc
ture. Germany, -Austria-Hungary, France
and Russia, it is stated, are now gather
ing a beet crop which will yield an output
of sugar far surpassing In quantity that
of any previous year. Unfortunately for
Germany, this overwhelming production
comes at a time when industrial depres
sion and a short wheat and rye crop have
seriously reduced the purchasing capacity
of the poorer classes. A high commercial
authority estimates the decline in sugar
consumption In Germany during the fiscal
year at 7000 tons.
"To complete and still further darken
the shadow," says the Consul General,
"Cuba has reappeared as a vigorous and
threatening competitor in the United
States, which during the past five years
has taken an average of 283,000 metric
tons of German sugar, valued at $12,614,000
per annum."
These conditions, It Is stated, have sent
the price of sugar in the market at
Madgeburg down to the lowest point
reached during the critical year 1894-95.
Great interest has been awakened in
Germany by the recent visit to Washing
ton of Governor General Wood of Cuba,
and the conviction is growing that, not
withstanding all doubts Â£nd suspicions,
Cuba is really about to begin the career
of an independent State under the gener
ous and sympathetic protection of the
United States and under conditions w.i.cn
will favor the development of the sugar
industry of the island to a point beyond
the highest productiveness of former
years.
Visiting Bishops Conduct Services.'
CINCINNATI. Nov. 3.â The Methodist
pulpits of this city. Covington, Newport
an i other Kentucky towns were tilled to
day by the visiting members of the
.Methodist Board of Bishops. Some of
the bishops preached three times during
the day, holding afternoon services, and
most of them preached twice during the
afternoon. To-morrow morning the ses
sions of the .board will be resumed and
final action taken on the reports and
other documents that have been sub
mitted to the various committees. The
conference of bishops will continue in
session until Wednesday.
Ship-Building Industry Grows.
WASHINGTON, Nov. 3. - The ship
building industry of the United States,
exclusive of the United States Navy
yards, according to a preliminary report
r i the Census Bureau, had a total Invent
ed capital of $76,699,651 In 1900.. This is an
increase of 181 per cent since 1890. The
value of products, including custom work
and repairing, was $73,444,753. an increase
of almost 93 per cent. There were 10S3
establishments and 40,121 wage earners
with total wages of $24,388,109, and miscel
laneous expenses of $3,582,257. The cost of
materials used was $33,031,280.

By Win. G. . Monday, 'Â¦ November 4, . at 7:45 p. m.,
Thoroughbred Yearlings, at 721 Howard street., ; ..." ; . :^ Â¦.
ON OLD-FASHIONED LINES.
FOR some weeks trade has been uniformly active
and featureless. It is .the same story, day
r.ftej day, of briskly moving merchandise, large
experts, Turn prices and general commercial content
ment throughout the land. Even Wall street, which
is the most sensitive spot in the country, has been
without a ripple, if the recent decline in the copper
stocks be excepted. The great staples have moved
along smoothly, passing from the farm^to the mer
chant, and thence to the consumer or to foreign
countries, without especial feature. The labor trou
bles have been left in the wake, and at the moment
the great commercial sea is as tranquil as a mill pond.
A glance at the country's bank clearings for the
past week tells the story. There was the usual
monotonous increase in the volume of trade over last
year, the gain being 25.3 per cent, not an important
city showing a decrease. The farlures were 172,
against 165 last year, and were mainly small and un
important. The iron and steel trade sent in the old
stereotyped reports of an active demand for struc
tural material, with the mills many months behind
their orders, and with new business being turned
down because it cannot be attended to with the
desired promptness. The manufacturers of footwear,
as intimated a fortnight ago, have been obliged to
make a small advance in their quotations, owing to
tlie higher prices for hides and leather, both of which
are active and firm. The textile mills are fully em
ployed, the woolen mills have all the orders they can
fill in the near future, and their purchases of raw
wool are keeping that product firm, the transactions
at Boston alone last week amounting to between
15,000,000 and 20,000,000 pounds.
If there be any distinctive feature to trade at the
moment it is the scarcity of cars, frequently men
tioned of late. This condition applies to all parts
of the United States and is hampering trade every
where. California is calling for cars to move its
grain, hay and fruit, and the coal regions of the East
are unable to meet the enormous domestic and large
) foreign demand for this fuel in cdnsequence of this
lack of transportation. The railroads are doing their
best, but cannot supply cars any faster than the
mills, rushed with all sorts of orders, can get the
material and build them. This condition shows the
general activity in trade throughout the country.
Were there sufficient cars to carry all the freight
offered, the business of the country would be phe
nomenal.
Another feature is the improvement in wheat and
corn, the latter leading in point of activity. The
Southwest is seriously short of this important staple,
and the shortage is effecting wheat and provisions.
The farmers in the Mississippi and Missouri valleys
are feeding large quantities of wheat to their stock
in lieu of the usual yellow staple, and provisions are
also feeling the effects of the shortage. Kansas is
buying corn in Missouri, and the latter State is
buying it in lowa, and much of the usual flow of
this grain to Chicago is being thus diverted to*the
Southwest. Germany is short 110,000,000 bushels of
wheat and* 37,000,000 bushels of rye, and France is
short of wheat, so between all these conditions the
grain market has been steadier than for some time,
though there is no excitement. There is plenty of
wheat in the world, apparently, for the English mar
kets decline to get excited over the current bullish
reports and fluctuate but little.
This is the general condition of trade, and it is
satisfactory. The major part of the world seems
to be carrying on business on old-fashioned lines,
without any more speculation than is necessary, with
plenty of money to meet its bills and with a fair
profit .on the goods. What more does it want?
THE DISTRICT ATTORNEY'S OFFICE
UNDER the new charter there is no more im
portant office than that of the District At
torney,
He has exclusive charge of all the criminal courts.
Without his consent no complaint can issue for the
arrest of any one. This power in the hands of a weak
or dishonest man would be a most severe menace to
the peace and welfare of our city.
The administration of the incumbent. Mr, Lewis F.
Byington, has been honest r capable and successful.
He should be re-elected.
FOR A "WXDE O^HSIT TOWN.
Â¦.Â¦â¢Â¦Â¦*-: -i?'?;- ;;-:tv~ â¢; .â¢â¢'.-;.Â¦/ v' Â¦ '.'.Â¦.Â¦>;.. "V;;- Â¦;'-Â¦<Â¦Â¦ \^ : - Â¦.-â¢ ;;-;-^-vf ; :.r ; ;, ;- : ? -â¢.â¢.,Â¦? .,:..,. ; ..Â¦Â¦:Â¦-.Â¦ :.- r .u Â¦;Â¦Â¦Â¦'Â¦Â¦: .?Â¦.' J v.. â¢:,Â¦: rÂ¦? 'Â¦'â¢â¢
WHEN Herrin and Jerc Burke, the Democratic railroad bosses, undertook to
dictate a yellow dog Supervisor ticket to the so called Republican Conven
tion in this city they were aware they could not make their fight alone.
The corporations back of Herrin and Burke are powerful, but not suffi
ciently so to dominate the city. It was necessary to have allies. Those allies could, not
be found among decent Republicans, and consequently they were sought among that
class of people who are in favor of running a wide open town in the interests of gam
blers, poolroom touts and the patrons of side door saloons.
The candidates for Supervisor nominated by the ''convention at the dictation of these
bosses are pledged not only to grant specified favors tÂ£ the corporations, but to do
whatever Herrin commands. The activity in the campaign of Gunst, Goldberg, Sam
Rainey and Dan Burns is ample warning to the people of what will happen if the cor
poration ticket be elected. We shall have a renewal of all those disgraceful vices whose
suppressor! cost the city suoh a prolonged fight. The poolrooms will, once more he
open for the enticement and the ruin not of men only but of bo^s and even of girls.
We shall have new stories of embezzlements, robberies; defalcations, murders and sui
cides. Dan Burns and his gang do . not do politics either for their own health or for
the health of the city. They are. in it for coin, and, in view of the frequent results of
their methods of getting coin, we might say they are in it for blood.
When they set about selecting candidates for Supervisors the conspirators chose men
most of whom are utterly unknown to the city at large. They bound those men to
specific pledges before they gave the nomination. The character of the men is such
that Mr. Wells, the nominee for Mayor, has himself said that had he known what sort
of men were to be nominated for Supervisor he would not have accepted a place, on the
ticket. It is such a set of men as attracts the support of Sam Rainey from the Demo
cratic camp and of Dan Burns from the obscurity in which he had been living ever since
he was beaten for the Senate. ' .
It is not for nothing that Burns, Gunst, Goldberg and Rainey have rallied to the sup
port of the Burke and Herrin ticket. No intelligent man can be so credulous as to sup
pose these men have been brought together by a mere accident of politics. They are
well known predatory politicians. They are out for loot. They have a ticket of Super
visors pledged to <\o what they wish, and if that ticket be elected San Francisco will
be the prey of gamblers, touts, toughs and every class of men who not only fatten upon
the vices of society but breed vices in order that they may fatten upon them.
We have then to face in the first place a seti of rapacious corporations seeking to
obtain unfair profits from their dealings with the city, and in the second place a set of
gamblers seeking an opportunity to ply their nefarious trades openly where every way
farer may be subject to their enticements and their deadfalls. It is an issue of honesty
and morality against dishonesty and immorality. It is a contest between those who are
seeking to give San Francisco a good government and those who are seeking to subvert
the government to the uses of vice.
There is not one defense to be made for the Burke and Herrin ticket. The men who
compose it are personally unfit to make laws for the city. The pledges they have given
to the corporations are in violation of the first duty of an honest candidate. The class
of bosses whom they have called to their support rouse the suspicions of the intelligent,
and the manner in which they are making their canvass by evasions, dodges and subter
fuges confirms the suspicions and infuses the community with a moral certainty thajt if
elected they will open the treasury and the streets to the corporations, and the city to
Burns and Gunst.
Against that ticket, whose members were selected solely because the conspirators be
lieved that in this campaign "yellow dogs" could win. there is in the field a body of can
didates nearly every one of whom has served on the Board of Supervisors and has proven
himself faithful, efficient, careful of the public interests and indisputably honest. Not a
single scandal has arisen out of the proceedings of this Board of Supervisors. Mr. Wells
himself has borne testimony to their worth and to the value of their service. They are
bound to no corporations nor to any bosses. No disreputable man is active in their
behalf. All the bosses and the* boodlers are against them. They stand forth as the
champions of law and order and decency. Their record is at once their platform and their
guarantee. They merit the support of every Republican as well as that of every Inde
pendent and every Democrat. There is no use to talk about partisanship in an affair of
this kind. Where such Republicans as Burns, Gunst and Goldberg are combined with
such Democrats as Herrin, Burke and Rainey, it is time for good citizens to unite to
save the city from the vultures.
BIT Fi^B:FRETE]SrSB;
DEVELOPMENTS of the campaign have brought to light the fact that the so.
called "Union Labor" party with its candidate, E. E. Schmitz, is making the
campaign largely by false pretense. The very title of the party is deceptive,
since it implies that the party is representative of organized labor, whereas it
represents nothing of the kind. Many of the strongest labor unions in the city have
denounced it, repudiated and emphatically declared that it does not in any way stand for
union labor principles or for the welfare of organized labor.
The protests of genuine labor unionism against the political adventurers who are. try
ing to get votes under the name of union labor have not been spoken hesitatingly 01 in
a corner. On the contrary they have been proclaimed with the utmost vigor and given
the widest publicity Organized Labor, a journal published by tl?e labor unions of San
Francisco, said to the union workers in its issue of Saturday:
If you desire to vote prosperity away from San Francisco; if you desire to vote to disband your
unions; if you want to vote the money out of your own. pockets; if you like to vote the bread away from
your wife and childrenâ then vote the class ticket, the worst class ticket, the Union Labor party ticket
and you will have accomplished all these. Yes, more, you will have put a nail into the coffin of trades
unionism as far as San Francisco is concerned for the next ten years. * * * History is replete with
such examples. Take the bleeding State of Kansas under its Populist administration, or any other com
monwealth or community where a serai-class party secured control of the reins of government. The result
is always the same. Capital has %gone into hiding and labor is begging for employment. Do you want
such a condition in San Francisco? Do you want your unions torn to pieces, your wages reduced to $o 75
a' day and your hours of work increased to ten or twelve? If so, vote the Labor ticket! Vote
against your union! Vote to disband! Vote yourselves into the same condition as those who voted with
Denis Kearney, who are now working on the outskirts of the city for their board and 25 cents a day
while the sandlot agitator is a millionaire. Vote away your independence and self-respect. Vote away
the p-osperity of the cityâ but remember that Organized Labor, as a trade union paper, gave you the
warning!
That warning uttered by Organized Labor shows what the genuine union working
men of the city think of the effort that is now beinj; made under the name of "Union
Labor" to intrude class antagonisms into municipal politics and to involve tabor
unions in political controversies. Class antagonism in politics would retard the progress
of the city and seriously injure all lines of industry, while the introduction of politics into
the^ unions would inevitably result in disrupting them.
While the party itself is thus making false pretense in calling itself by the name and
title of union labor, the candidate for Mayor, Mr. Schmitz, is no better than his party.
Responsible union workingmen have charged him with playing double during the strike,
and of violating the principles of unionism in His dealings with non-union shops. The
charges have been supported by evidence which has been published in The Call, and they
remain unrefuted. Thus Mr. Schmitz no more than the. men who nominated him is a
representative of true unionism. He is the representative only of the disturbing ele
ments of the community. His election would encourage every demagogue, every agita
tor and every discontented blatherskite to at once set about trying to cause new strikes
new class antagonisms and new outbreaks of violence. ,
False as are the pretenses under which the Schmitz campaign has been conducted, it is
none the less formidable. It is sure that as soon a* the advocates of a wide open town
perceive that the defeat of Wells is certain, they will swing their vote for Schmitz
There is but one way to save San Francisco from the double danger menaces^
and that is for every good citizen, every genuine labor unionist, every taxpayer every
opponent of corporate corruption and of unlimited gambling to vote for Xobin and for
his Supervisor ticket.
Henry Watterson is reported to have assured the
temperance people that they will have no occasion to
complaiji of the use of wine at the White House
should he be elected President, so it looks as if Henry
had started his campaign in earnest and expects to
run on an anti- Bourbon platform.

thing of a swindle and that there ought not to be any

GIRLS OF FLORODORA DOUBLE
SEXTET SELECT STUNNING GOWNS
IHAVE found out what the Doctor's Daug>ters* swell
"Florodora" double sextet girls are going to wear. The
whole thing is supposed to be a dead secret, but the girls,
naturally excited over fetching gowns, stunning hats, swell
shoes and "just the dearest boas In the world," got to tell
ing a few "nearest and .dearest" about Itâ and it came to my
ears. At first I was not going to divulge tho secret, and then
I realized how anxious the public was to know It and how very
anxious I was to tell it.
Well, the gowns are dreams. They are of white crepe,
made perfectly plain and clinging with a twelve-inch accordeon
pleated ruffle around the skirt, which is cut en train.. The
sleeves read, only to the elbows, and the corsage is finished off
with a "baby neck." The hats are, of course, the regulation
Florodora hatsâ all black and covered with nodding black
plumes. Long black gloves will be worn and dainty "colonial"
ties. The most fetching feature of the costumes, I think, is
the pink boas, with long streamers and big pink roses. All the
girls in the sextet are pretty. And with that costume onâ
â¢ â¢ â¢
Now that the girls have settled on their costumes, I under
stand that the men are passing through the same form of
troubleâ what to wear. The vexed problem with them Is, Shall
it be frock coats or Tuxedos and manilas or straw sailors or
silk hats? It lÂ« a mighty question to decide, and I know that
the "gentle strangers" are doing some pretty hard thinking.
In the meantime rehearsals are going on every day, and
Bothwcll Browne, who Is rehearsing the sextet, has taken to
drilling tho couples individually. Hard work and close atten
tion is the slogan these days, and there is not the slightest
doubt in my mind that the society amateurs will outshine the.
professionals who lately charmed us at the Columbia.
â¢ -. â¢ â¢
I understand that a surprise verse is to be sprung, by the
sextet. George Carr has "dropped Into poetry" and has reeled
off a verse that is witty, snappy and telling, and .which deals
out little love pats to the Bohemians who are to take part in
"The Billionaire."

The Marguerite Auxiliary of the Children's Hospital gave
a delightful dance on Saturday evening at Cotillon Hall. It
was an Invitational affair, but all who attended paid SO cents
at the door, and the sum thus collected was turned over to the
hospital. '
The officers of the auxiliary are: Miss Ruth Merrill, presi
dent; Miss Carol Moore, vice president; Miss Merritt Reid, sec
ond vice president; Miss Helen Ashton, secretary; Miss Maud
Payne, corresponding secretary; Miss Kathleen Bull, treasurer.

INTERESTING FACTS RELATIVE TO THE GROWING
NAVIES OF THE GREAT POWERS OF THE WORLD
THE Russian battleship Retvizan, built at Cramps' yard,
is practically completed. The builders have not only
turned out a faster ship than the contract called for,
but they have also built 4t in better time than any other
contractor for similar vessels. The keel of the Retvlsan
was laid November 5, 1898; the launching took place October 23,
1900, and the steam trial October 23, 1901. The time in building
and completing this 12,700-ton battleship, Including delivery of
armor, guns and other requisites, was therefore a little less
than two years between the laying of the keel and the launch
ing, and exactly one year between launch and trial, making a
total of practically three years' time. The three battleships
Maine, Missouri and Ohio, building for the United States navy
at Cramps', Newt Drt News and the Union Iron Works, do not
show the same speed in construction. They were contracted
for in October, 1898, to be completed as follows during the pres
ent year: Maine, June 1; Ohio, June 5, and Missouri, August
30. The latter is still on the stocks, the Ohio was launched
May 18 last and the Maine July 27. On October 1 last the Maine
was 67 per cent advanced toward completion, the Missouri 45
per cent and the Ohio only 43 per cent. At this rate it is cal
culated that the Maine may be turned over to the Government
in October, 1962, and the other two ships ln ( February, 1908. That
is to say, they will be from fifteen to twenty months behind
their contract date of delivery. The chief cause for this delay
is due to the armor manufacturers, who tried to overreach the
Government, and consequent waste in time to bring them to
reasonable terms. There was no such trouble with the armor
for the Russian ship, nor v.-ere the contractors hampered with
the red tape interference by Government officials which greatly
retards building of vessels for the United States navy.
The Russian battleship Tsarevitch, 12,900 tons, building in
France, was contracted for simultaneously with the Retvizan
in the United States, but is still far from completion. .Her keel
was not laid until May, 1899, the launch took place February 23
last and her trial wIM not come off before July, next year.

SUNDAY CONTRACTSâ A. O. .8., City.
Contracts made on Sunday are popularly
supposed to be void. But' this is a matter
that largely depends on local statutory
law. In the absence of a statutory inhibi
tion, parties may make a valid contract
on Sunday as well as on any other day.
POLL TAXâ Exempt, Cloverdale, Cal.
Previous to the adoption of the present
constitution of California exempt firemen
414 not have to pay poll tax. The reason
that the officials of Sonoma County col
lect such tax- is because the constitution
authorises them to do so. If the exempt
fireman is over 60. years of age he la not
required to pay the tax:" Active members
or exempt members of a duly organised
fire company .are exempt from jury duty.

A serviceable class of gunboats, or sloops as they are de
nominated in England, Is building for the British navy. Four
have been contracted for and four are in course of construction
at the dockyards. They are of 980 tons displacement, 1400 horse
power and 13.25 knots speed, and carry a battery of six 4-inch
quick-firers and four 3- pounders. The Rinaldo. built by Lairds,
at a cost of $335,000, made 13% knots on her trial last month.
Four of these sloops are sheathed with wood and coppered,
which, in conjunction with their light draught, make this class
of vessels very serviceable in tropical seas and particularly
useful for navigation of rivers and shallow waters. It is the
type recommended by our Navy Department for service in the
Philippines and of which a number will undoubtedly be built
in the near future.
â¢ * â¢
In France and Russia all mercantile ships are at the dis
posal of the Government in time of war. In Germany but
eighty steamers are subsidized for war purposes, and in Great
Britain forty-eight only are subject to Government use by vir
tue of* subsidies. France pays annually $1,160,000 in subsidy for
ship, construction and $2,440,000 on navigation. Neither England,
Germany, Russia nor the United States pays any ship-building
subsidy, and the . sums granted for navigation are $337,500 by
Germany, $1,639,475 by Russia, including the volunteer fleet, and
$330,000 by Great Britain, granted to the West India and Cana
dian trades. *
â¢ ? ?
THe harbor of Emden on the North Sea is being deepened
with a view of accommodating vessels of the greatest draught.
The outer harbor is to be dredged to a depth of 38 feet and the
inner harbor from the mouth of the river Ems will be 33 feet
deep for a distance of ten miles. The harbor is open through
out the year and will be serviceable for the largest steamers
and war vessels.
â¢ ? â¢
In the matter of coaling stations Russia is the most defi
cient of all' naval powers at the present time, as along the
ocean highway from St. Petersburg or Odessa to Port Arthur
she has not a single coal bunker. This important question is
now receiving the attention of the Government and places ar%
looked for In the Mediterranean, the Red Sea and the Persian
Gulf.
The French torpedo-boat No. 124 was sunk last month in
collision with another torpedo-boat. No. 139, off the island of
Corsica. The bulkhead forward of the engine room gave way,
tilling the boat. She belonged to the third class, was buht in
1890 and rated at 20 knots.

DRAMATIC SCENE
IN A CATHEDRAL
Excommunicated Catho
lic Priest Is Publicly
Humiliated.
He Refuses to Leave Sacred
Edifice and Service Is
Stopped.
CHICAGO. Nov. 3.â There was an. in
Â¦ tensely dramatic scene in Holy Name
Cathedral to-day, when in the presence of
fully 1000 parishioners Jeremiah J. Crow
ley, the excommunicated Roman Catholic
priest, was publicly humiliated by order
of the authorities of the church.
Father Crowley had entered the church
unobserved, passing up the center aisle,
; and had taken a seat almost under the
j pulpit.. Solemn high mass was being cele
brated at the time. When the presence
cf Father Crowley became known Rev.
Francis J. Barry, chancellor- of the arch
diocese of Chicago, was hurriedly sent
for. Upon'entering the cathedral he went
straight to the seat where the excommuni
cated clergyman was kneeling and or
dered him to leave the church. Father
Crowley refused to go, saying:
"Put me out if you dare."
The strain was intense, and one woman
in the congregation fainted. There was
no resort to force, however. Chancellor
Barry signaled to a man in the choir lott,
and the sound of the organ ceased and
the singing of the choir was hushed. The
priests in the altar stopped the solemn
service at the end of the "Gloria", and
walked to the benches and laid aside their
golden vestments. The altar boys marched
out of the sanctuary through a side door
and the priests, clad in their cassocka,
followed. The next moment the hundreds
of incandescent lights in the vaulted
arches were extinguished and the candle
lights on the altar were put out.
The strain was broken when Chancellor
Barry appeared in the pulpit and said:
"Owing to- the preserice in this sacred
edifice of an excommunicated priest the
solemn high mass has been suspended.
We will proceed with a low mass."
No sermon was delivered, however, and
the mass was a,t an end before the congre
gation was calm again. The parishioners
lingered around the cathedrar and
watched the deposed priest as he hurried
away. None spoke to him.
Sunday at Park and Beach.
Thousands of people visited the park
and ocean beach yesterday. It was long
after dark before the last of the crowd of
pleasure seekers returned home. Sutro
Baths were crowded during the day. Fol
lowing are the results of the aquatic con
tests:
Fifty-yard dash, juvenilesâ J. Sundberg first,
J. Erasmy second.

Closkey second.
Hundred-yard dash, amateursâ T. Sundberg
first, J. Erasmy second.

second. J. MeCloskey third.

A. Sundberg second.-

Sundberr second.
