HUSBAND RUNS
AS WIFE BURNS
Overturned Stove Causes
Possible Fatal
Injuries.
Mrs. Bessie Overton, who resided with
! her husband at 640 Golden Gate avenue,
was fatally burned yesterday afternoon
in her apartments. She lighted a coal oil
stove about 1 o'clock to heat the room and
in passing overturned it. The oil ignited
and set the apartment on lire. Instead of
going to his wife's assistance, Overton mn
out to turn in an alarm of fire.
While Mrs. Overton was attempting to
put out the blaze her dress took lire .and
before assistance could reach her, she
was probabty fatally burned. Louis Ven
ner and his wite, who occupy the adjoin
ing apartments, attracted by the woman's
screams, rushed to her aid. -Mrs. Ven
ner threw a blanket about her and ex
tinguished the fire. She then took her in
to her own apartments and gave her such
treatment as was possible. Later Mrs.
Overton was removed to the Central
Emergency Hospital, where she was treat- !
ed by Dr, Morrison. Examination dls- |
closed the fact that not a single por
tion of her body had escaped the flames.
He said that he could give no hope for
her recovery.
The fire was extinguished by the depart
ment and was confined to the room in
which it started. The house is conducted
by Mrs. Ada Hunsacker and is occupied
by a number of families. The Overtons
have resided there for a couple of years.
The husband Is employed as a marker in
the Golden Gate laundry.
Mrs. Overton was formerly a Miss Loft
house and resided in Virginia City, Nev.
Her father was burned to death when she
was a small child. An aunt. Mrs. Har
den, resides at Steamboat Springs, near
Virginia City and has been notified. A
brother is connected with one of the in
fantry regiments stationed at the
Presidio.
STAB CHINESE WOMAN
AS RESULT OF FEUD
Highbinders Assault Merchant's Wife
as She Is About to Enter
Her Home.
Leong Kee, the 18-year-old wife of
Chlng Kee, a merchant at 716 Dupont
street, was stabbed last night by some
unknown Chinese as she was about to en
ter her home at 822 Dupont street. She
had been visiting at the Chinese consulate
and had reached the second flight of
stairs when two Chinese wearing masks
stepped out of the hallway and one of
them plunged a dagger Into her right side
The woman screamed and the Chinese
fled, dropping the dagger in their flight.
The police were immediately notified and
a search- was made for the would-be as
sassins, but no trace of them could be
found. The wound is serious, but not
necessarily fatal.
The stabbing is believed to be the out
come of the murder of a member of the
Ching farrtily by a member of the Won?
family in Buck Oye's gambling place a
few nights ago. The members of the
Chlng family met last night with the Six
Companies and demanded the lives of five
members of the Won* family as a com
pensation for the death of the lookout.
Chlng Kee Is connected with the Wonjr
family, and this is supposed by the police
to be the first attempt In retaliation.
Police Officers Herllhy, Bonner and
Morton began a search for the woman's
assailants soon after the occurrence, but
were unable to find any 'trace of" them.
CONQUEST OF CUPID IS SEALED
UNDER FLOWERING WEDDING BELL
Hundreds of Prominent Southern California
People Attend an Impressive Ceremony
in the Masonic Temple at Ventura
VENTURA. Nov. 3.â The Masonic
TCmple Iff -thftncity was crowded
to the doors to-day with a throng
of prominent society people of
Southern California to witness
the marriage of Miss Rose Roth to Philip
A. Newmark, of Los Angeles.
yVhile the guests were being seated by
the ushers Angeloty's orchestra, of Los
Angeles, rendered several selections.
When the ribbons had â¢ been stretched
along both sides of the aisles the wed
ding march from "Lohengrin" was played,
and from the left ante-room came the
maid of honor and bridesmaids. The
groomsmen entered from the right ante
room. All met at the main aisle and
formed the bridal party. The bride was
followed by her parents and M. A. New
mark and Mrs. Casper Cohn, brother and
sister of the groom.
As the bride reached the head of the
aisle the groom and best man, who came
from the chapel room, met her and escort
ed her before the officiating rabbi, S.
Hecht, of Los Angeles. The ceremony
was performed under a mammoth flower
BUBONIC PLAGUE
IS DISAPPEARING
No New Oases Reported
at Liverpool or
Glasgow.
LIVERPOOL, Nov. 3.â No further ca3eK
of the bubonic plague in Liverpool were
officially reported to-day. United States
Consul Boyle assures intending passenger
for the United States that they need not
fear for the present time that they w.Â»l
be quarantined on reaching American
ports. Â¦ Speaking last evening at a local
hospital banquet Boyle said:
"Realising my responsibility for such a
declaration, I assert that the city of LÂ»iv->
erpool does more business with the United
States than the rest of the world together.
I can say that I am not a bit nervous
over this outbreak of the plague in Liv
erpool, if I thought the situation serious
enough to require the quarantine of ves
sels from Liverpool at Ijnited States ports
I would recommend it, but thus far I
have not seen any such necessity."
WASHINGTON, Nov. 3.â There were no
advices to-day to the Marine Hospital
Service from its representatives in Liver
pool or Glasgow regarding the bubonic
plague. The officials will exert every ef
fort to prevent the introduction of the
plague Into the United States to which
end there will be earnest co-operation be
tween the national and State authorities.
Already there are in existence regulations
for the examination of persons suspected
of suffering with plague symptoms and
these will be supplemented if necessary.
Tempest in a Coffee-Pot.
An overcharged coffee pot in the Palms
restaurant, on Market street, sent Pan
tryman A. Stevenson to the Emergency
Hospital yesterday for txeatment for
divers burns. The pantryman was In the
act of lifting the pot from the stove when
it exploded in his hand, throwing the con
tents over his face, neck and arms. A*
none of the exploding coffee was sent to
the hospital Dr. Morrison was unable to
determine the explosive Ingredients, but
he dressed Stevenson's injuries and sent
him back to bis dangerous place of labor, I
Bobbed at Cards.
N. E. Boyna. clerk on the transport
Grant, .reported to the police yesterday
that he had been robbed of $115 by a
stranger who had inveigled him into a
game of cards in a saloon at O'Fanrell
and Stockton streets. From a descrip
tion given of the fellow by Boyne, Detec
tive Ryan and Policeman Hayden a short
time afterward arrested a well dressfti
young man, who gave his name as C. B.
Sylvester, and charged him with the
crime. Sylvester claims that he is a vic
tim of mistaken identity.

cles. She spent the past two winters in
Los Angeles, where she participated in
many important social functions. She is
highly accomplished, especially In music
and elocution. The groom is a prominent
young business man of Los Angeles.' '

ding and about 300 the reception. A spe

VILE SLANDERER
GOES TO PRISON
Court-Martial Imposes
Heavy Sentence on
Naval Employe.
WASHINGTON, Nov. 3.â To be confined
one year in such place as the Secretary of
the Navy may direct tc do extra police
duty during that time; to lose all pay
except $2 a month for prison expenses and
the further sum of $30 to be paid him at
the expiration of this confinement, and to
be dishonorably discharged from the
service of the United States is the sen
tence imposed by court-martial upon John
W. Stoll, a third-class electrician of the
receiving ship Columbia, lying at the New
York navy-yard, for foul abuse of the late
President McKiiney.
On the date of the late President's death
Stoll in the company of a number of sail
ors is charged with having declared with
an obscene oath that the President
should have been shot long ago and to
have added other foul and profane ex
pressions of the same tenor. He was
arrested and tried by court-martial with
the result above announced on two
charges: First, conduct to the prejudice
of good order and discipline; second,
scandalous conduct tending to the de
struction of good morals.
Stoll made a vehement plea of inno
cence, declaring that his language re
ferred to the President's assassin, but the
evidence of the bystanders and the lan
guage Itself was regarded by the court as
completely establishing his guilt. He will
be confined in the prison ship Southery at
Norfolk. .

STEAMER BRINGS
MAN OF MYSTERY
London's Indian "Prince"
Guest of a Hotel in
Montreal.
Arrives With Suite of Twenty-
Bight Persons, Includ
ing Dancing Girl.'
SpeelU Dispatch to Tht Ctll.
MONTREAL, Nov. 3.â Among the pas
sengers on the Elder-Dempster steam
ship Lake Simcoe, which arrived in this
port at 4 o'clock this afternoon, was a
mysterious person who styled himself
Prince Ranjit of Baluchistan. He was
accompanied by a dashing suite of twen
ty-eight persons, including a dancing girl,
whose special mission is to while away
the tedium of life. Who the stranger is
is a mystery. The India authorities have
issued an official notice that there is no
BUch Prince, but whether a real Prince or
an impostor he travels as a nabob. He
was the talk of London for several weeks,
during which Lime he stopped at one of
the swell hotels, where he had twenty
three rooms and spent money lavishly.
The mystery as to the man's identity re
mained untathomed and ia still as deep as
ever. He declines to see newspaper men
or even hold conversation with other
people, his only reply to questions being,
â¢ i am a Kin*?."
He is* a man of fine physique, dark
skinned and handsome. I'he fame of the
mysterious person bad preceded him, and
when the Lake Simcoe steamed Into port
this afternoon there was quite a crowd oÂ£
interested spectators on the wharf wait
ing to catch a glimpse of him. They were
doomed to disappointment. The Prince
remained secluded in his stateroom. It
was said he had spent nearly the whole
of the voyage in his room and suffered a
good deal from seasickness. He, however,
patronized a concert that was given on
board and contributed $300 to the collec
tion. His dancing girl was present and
gave a novel entertainment. During the
rest of the time he remained in his room,
where he had all his meals brought to him
by hia attendant.
The Prince and his suite, when most
of the crowd had dispersed, took carriages
and proceeded to the Windsor Hotel. Ha
intends to make a tour of the principal
points in the United States and Canada,
and will return to London in time to be
present at the King's coronation.
BUSINESS MEN
EXCHANGE VIEWS
Prepare to Consider the
Value of Commercial
Reciprocity.
NEW YORK, Nov. 3.â Members of the
New York Board of Trade and Transpor
tation have Issued a call for a special
meeting to consider "commercial reciproc
ity."
The call is addressed to members of the
board who are engaged in manufacturing.
The call reads as follows:
In view of the active discussion of the tend
ency and practicability of the broader appli
cation of the people or commercial reciprocity
a a means of expanding f orelgn markets for
American products without sacrificing the in
terests of any of our Industries, the under
signed, with the approval of the executive
committee of the board,- invite and request
all members of the Board of Trade and Trans
portation who are engaged in manufactures
to designate a principal or executive officer in
each firm or corporation to meet in the board's
rooms on Thursday, November 7, at 2:30 p. m.,
for a liberal comparison of views on the sub
ject to formulate some expression of the senti
ment of the meeting, and if deemed desirable,
to select and recommend to the board ten dele
gates to the National Reciprocity Convention,
which will be held in Washington on Novem
ber W.
The undersigned are aware that differing
views of the value of commercial' reciprocity
are held among manufacturers and have not
ourselves compared opinions
The meeting, therefore, is called absolutely
without prejudice or bias and those attending
will shape conclusions.
William Carroll of the American Leather
Company; the Eaton, Cole & Burhan Com
pany, E. H. Cole, treasurer; the Mergenthaler
Linotype Company. P. T. Dodge, president;
H. J. Baker & Brother, by W. D. Far is; the
National Lead Company, R. P. Rowe, man
ager; Charles A. Scbieren of Charles A. Schie
ren Ik Co., and Frank S. Gardener, the secre
tary of the board.
REVENUE CUTT.EB, BEAU
COMING TO THIS PORT
SEATTLE, Wash., Nov. 3.â The reve
nue cutter Bear arrived in port to-nigTit
after discharging ballast at the Puget
Sound Navy Yard. She sails at noon to
morrow for San Francisco, going first to
Coraax for coal. Captain Tuttle's orders
are to load his bunkers to their capacity,
which is taken here as an indication that
the Bear may be sent on to winter at
Honolulu.
The Coast and Geodetic Survey steamer
Gedney, which has arrived from summer
work In Alaskan waters, is\ordered to
make hydrographic surveys and sound
ings in San Juan channel of Puget Sound!
After the completion of this work the
Gedney will return to Seattle to have a
new set of boilers installed.

WASPS GARNER
DOUBLE-HEADER
Win Last Two Games
of the Series With
Sacramento.
Inglorious Defeat for the Sen
ators at Recreation
Park.
The Senators wound up the present se
ries at Recreation Park yesterday after
noon by suffering inglorious defeat at the
bands of the Wasps. The score was 16
to 8. Of these runs the victors rolled up
a total of nine in the fourth inning after
one man was out. For a time it seemed
the inning never would end. It was not
good baseball, but it was interesting to
watch. ,<
Five pitchers appeared in the box at
different times. Uncle Henry giving Krug,
Whalen and Iburg an airing, while Doyle
and Hoft'er represented the Senators.
The amiable Mr. Flood of Sacramento
was escorted off the field in the sensa
tional fourth inning by a policeman be
cause of his unnecessary objections to
Umpire Harper's judgment on balls and
strikes.
Krug picked out a ball that suited him
in the eighth and sent it sailing like a
ruraway balloon over the center field
fence for a home run. He was the first
batter uy.
The Wasps settled down to business in
the first inning, after losing two men.
Wilson singled; Hildebrand and Pabst
walked, filling the bases. Krug hit to
shortstop. Devereaux had to run to get
the ball and instead of catching it he
bumped it with his knee, sending it roll
ing toward right field. Before it could oe
fielded Wilson and Hildebrand scored and
Krug reached second. Shay followed with
a two-bagger to the left field fence, scor
ing Pabst and Krug. Riley new out, re
tiring the side.
In the fourth the Wasps landed on
Doyle for six hits, on which, as
sisted by errors, they scored nine
runs. Shay opened proceedings by
hitting safe. Reiily bunted and was
safe on the catcher's fumble. Gra
ham sacrificed, advancing the men to sec
ond and third. Nordyke's timely two
bagger sent them home safely. Wilson
singled, scoring Nordyke. Hildebrand hit
safely and Schwartz was hit by pitcher,
again filling the bases. Pabst hit the
center field fence. This, with a passed
ball by Stanley scored three men. Pabst
scored immediately afterward on Krug's
three-bagger. Shay walked and "Heine"
scored on a "double steal." Riley scored
Shay on an infield hit. Graham struck
out and Wilson was out at first. This
ended what gave promise of becoming a
continuous performance.
"Jimmy the Whale" surprised every
body by lining the ball out along the
right field foul line in the sixth, scoring
Shay and Reiily. The batter was out try
ing to make third on the play. Krug's
home run in the eighth ended the Wasp's
run getting.
Aided by Shay/'s fumble and a wild
throw by Whalen, the Senators scored
three men in the seventh. It was only a
flash and ended with that. They had pre
viously sent five men home. The com
plete score:

MORNING GAME.
Wasps Win Handily.
The "Wasps had no difficulty account*
ing for the morning game by a score of
7to 2. Nordyke made a sensational catch
of a hit by Flood, which would have been
a three-bagger. Nordyke ran Into the
fence in catching it and was knocked
down. He held the ball. Sacramento
tried out Brown, an amateur pitcher.
The Wasps secured fourteen hits off his
delivery. The score:

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, MONDAY, NOVEMBER 4, 1901.
GUS RUHLIN, THE CENTRAL FIGURE IN THE TRAINING CAMP AT BLANKEN'S SIX-MILE HOUSE. AND
TWO MEN WHO ARE INTERESTED IN HIS TRAINING, ONE KEEPING HIM IN EXERCISE, THE OTHER
SUPPLYING MUSCLE PRODUCING FOOD IN LIBERAL QUANTITIES.
"Billy" Delaney May Insist on Jeffries Remaining at Harbin
Springs Until the Day Before the Fight.
VISITORS 1 day was observed
strictly yesterday at the train
ing quarters of Gus Ruhlln. From
early morning the road leading
â¢ to Blanken's was dotted with pe
destrians of sporting proclivities eager to
see for themselves the condition of the
man who aspires to be champion of the
wprld.
Ruhlin, in accordance with a life-long
custom, attended church in the morning.
He walked from Blanken's to All Hal
lows' Church, South San Francisco, and
after services strolled leisurely back over
the long road.
The one important incident of the day
was the trying on of the gloves which
Gus will wear in his battle with the
champion. Sol Levinson. the glove
maker, arrived at the training quarters
shortly after noon with a pair of 1 fighting
gloves which he had made especially for
the big fellow. Levinson had taken Ruh
lin's hand measurements earlier in the
week and the gloves which he produced
yesterday fitted the Akron fighter's hands
to a nicety. Gus tried on the gloves, as
sumed a fighting attitude and gripped
them thoroughly before expressing satis
faction. He took them off and examined
them carefully for flaws but found none.
"They're just my size," said Gus, as he
gave them a parting squeeze and handed

them to Levinson. According to mutual
agreement the gloves will be placed in
charge of Referee Harry Corbett, who
will see that nobody tampers with them
before the fight.
Jeff's gloves were sent to his training
quarters for fit and inspection Saturday.
If they are satisfactory, one of Jeffs
trusted followers will be delegated to
place them in the hands of Harry Cor
bett. Gloves may be easily tampered
with before a tight, and for this reason
the referee will be Instructed to keep an
unusually watchful eye upon them.
There have been several incidents in the
history of prize fighting where gloves
have been so weakened by removing the
stitching that they would break and new
gloves would be substituted. In the ex
citement the substitutes would not be
carefully inspected and gross injustice
and frauds were perpetrated. Neither the
principals in the coming fight fror their
managers would stoop to such practices,
but if outsiders were allowed to handle
the gloves some question of fraud might
be raised.
As both contestants have agreed to
wear no bandages of any kind on their
hands the gloves were made snug and
close-fitting. They are the regulation five
ounce glovts. with no finger holes in the
point. A tough ridge of leather running
across the point of the gloves is used as
a substitute for finger holes. This allows
the fingers free play and the blood free
circulation, thus obviating the danger of
cramps.

After the glovemaker finished with Ruh
lin the big fellow settled down to enjoy
his day of rest. He lounged about the
veranda of the training quarters ami
chatted with his trainers, Manager
"Billy" Madden and visitors. He talked
everything but tight and showed a dis
position to discourage any conversation
that had any bearing on the subject of
training or lighting. Gus has been at
Blanken's nearly nine weeks.
HARBIN SPRINGS, Nov. 3.-Jeffries
rested to-day. His trainers were glad, for
they were very tired from Saturday's
work. On Saturday night Jeffries put
them through a course of sprouts that
took all the curl out of their hair. The
champion worked in the gymnasium at a
fast clip. In the morning he boxe.i,
punched the bag, skipped the rope, played
four games of handbail with The Call rep
resentative, handled the medicine ball and
worked on the rowing machine. In thu
afternoon he took a ten-mile jaunt on the
road.
Billy Delaney, the famous trainer, who
is looking after the big fellow, returned
from the city yesterday. He has been ab
sent four days arranging terms for the
kinetoscope rights for the big fight. The
champion missed his trainer and was glad
to see him back. From now on Delaney
will devote his attention exclusively to
Jeffries' work. He is unwilling to let the
big fellow go to the city on Friday night.
He is afraid the change of air and diet
will make him stale. It begins to look
now as if the champion would stay at
Harbin until the day before the big battle.

Joe Bernstein to Wrestle.

and wrestler, has undertaken to throw
James Johnson, a sailor weighing 148
pounds, twice in one hour to-nufrrow
to be catch-as-catch-can, strangle hold
not barred. George Mlehllng VjlTreferee.

NAVAL RESERVE
AT THE BUTTS
Marksmen Show Skill at
Quarterly State
Shoot.
Other Organizations Hold
Customary Monthly
Contests.
The quarterly State shoot of the Naval
Reserve was the event of greatest inter
est yesterday at the Shell Mound ranges.
The attendance was good and the con
tests were for the most part close and ex
citing.
Dr. T. W. B. Leland, as usual, defeated
all comers with the revolver and made
one perfect string. Seaman A. Skaggs
won the officers' medal for proficiency
with the rifle at 200, 300 and 500 yards,
with a score of 211. Master at Arms Bro
phy was a close second, with 205.
The Oakland Turner Schuetzen Section,
which is composed of business men from
Oakland, made its first appearance at the
range yesterday and shot creditably.
The other organizations which held con
tests were companies D and F, First In
fantry, National Guard of 'California;
Deutscher Krieger Verein and the Inde
pendent Rifles.

FENII PROVES
COURSING WONDER
Geary's Greyhound Pup
Runner-Up in the
Open Stake.
Three Events at Union Park
Result in Good Card
of Ooursing.
Eugene Geary's Hughie-imp. Bonnie
Lass greyhound puppy Fenli made a sen
sational showing in the open stake yester
day at Union Coursing Park. After beat
ing some fast coursers he finished as run
ner-up to his more experienced kennel
mate Ireland.
The talent had a bad day, as many of
the high-priced choices went wrong
Fenii did much to add to the discomfiture
of the wise ones and lost them their
money on three occasions. In the second
round Geary's youngster brought 4 to 1
for her backers by beating Chiarini Bros.'
White Hat. In his next time out he out
pointed Curtis' Kibosn, ?. 5 to 4 favorite.
Hickory Dick was the next to go against
the winner, and though the talent figured
Fenil had about shot his bolt and laid 7 to
2 against his chances, the new wonder
showed even better form in this go ami
defeated the Futurity runner-up without
allowing him to score.
J). J. Healey's Fine Form also proved a
had one for the talent to lay odds against.
On two occasions he beat a 5 to 1 favor
ite. King Cotton in the second round and
Vulcan ki the next.
In the special stake Narcissus took first
money, beating Homer Boy handily in the
final.

COLOMBIA'S CHIEF
Carry Aged President
Sanclemente Into
Captivity.
Venerable Head^ of the Re
public Taken From His
Home in Villeta.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
HONDA, Sept. 30, via New York, Nov.
3.â Dr. Manuel Antonio Sanclemente, the
venerable President of the republic of Co
lombia, has been kidnaped by orders of
political leaders, who had already
stripped him of power. Locked in a
guardo a miserable parody on a sedan
chair in which courtiers once took their
ease, he is being hurried over rough
mountain roads from his home in Villeta.
department cf Cundlnamarca, to his beau
4<ful hacienda known as Pichihiat, in the
"partment of Cauca. He must be ea~
i^d through the very heart of two great
of the Andes, passing Honda,
-anizales, Cartago, Tulua and Buga,
twns which gave him ovations while h-.e
executive power. The greatest
has been observed and every ef
*rt has been made to prevent the facts
tconr.ng public for fear that friends and
p ies of the deposed President might at
\npt to rescue him. No paper in the re
iblic to-day would dare to print the bare
*:t that Sanclemente had lett Villeta,
Hch less that he waa a prisoner.
Firm in His Attitude.
'he only charge that can be made
Mnst Sanclemente Is that while he re-
VLaeii at Villeta he was a constant men
* to these in control of the government
Hbogota. He had never varied in hir.
1 Hion since the day when he was rele
Â£ed to the background and the Vice
wident assumed executive powerâ July
&190). He maintained that this action
h been unconstitutional and that the
lVers of the Presidency could no more
fclahen from him than the title of Pres
int. Ministers representing foreign
aernments, after long refusing to rec
tfize Jose Manuel Marroquin as execu
zx lead of the nation, gave in and left
t President in his retirement,
'illcta, through Sanclemente's residence
re, has stood In much the same rela
5 toward Bogota that Versailles occu
il toward Paris in the- empire. Fashion-
i s e I'ersor.s who had flocked there to ba
Âear the President, as well as to escape
the boon; rigorous climate of the canita!.
suddenly forsook it, knowing that the ex
isting complications rendered casual com
munications with Sanclemente dangerous.
It had been assumed by those who man
aged the coup of July, 1900, that Sancle
mente's chagrin at his loss of power would
Itrad him to leave the neighborhood of Bo
gota. The old gentleman merely stood his
ground, continuing to regard himself as
President and awaiting the moment when
he would be recalled to authority. Those
about him insist that he took no steps
against Vice President Marroquin' s gov
ernment and that he scorned any form of
count erplot.
Rebels Are Still Active.
The plea on which power had been taken
from him was that he was superannuated
and unable longer to discharge the duties
of his office. Dr. Marroquin, upon whom
the executive power was conferred, is in
appearance very nearly as old.
The revolution begain in October, 1899,
and lasted nine months. While Sancle
mente was President de facto his troops
had destroyed the Liberal armies, but
there were grave complaints because the
country had not been entirely pacified.
Dr. Marroquin has had charge for nearly
fifteen months and no apparent progress
has b=en made. Armed bands still roam
through the interior and the skirmishes
that occur daily tend merely to demon
strate the vitality of the rebels. Bulletins
are posted daily on the walls of Bogota
and republished in little newspapers, an
nouncing great victories for the Govern
ment, here, there and everywhere.

NATIVES SAVE
LOST MARINER
Sole Survivor of Halcyon
Tells Story of Suf
fering.
Long Journey in the Frozen
Wilds Nearly Ends in
Tragedy.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
TACOMA, Wash., Nov. 3.â A thrilling
tale of suffering and rescue in the nick
of time is told by Captain Tuttle of the
revenue cutter Bear, which is to sail for
San Francisco via Comox, B. C. It con
cerns William Egan, sole survivor of the
schooner Halcyon, which sailed from
Nome last November.
Off Sledge Island the Halcyon was
caught in an ice field which drifted
northward until January 8 last, when
land was sighted forty miles away. By
this time the entire crew had died except
Captain Thomas and Egan. They started
for land, but Captain Thomas gave out
and tried to return to his imprisoned
vessel. He has never since been heard
of. Egan concluded that the land signted
was St. Lawrence Island and started for
an Indian village on its further shore.
Eg^an's provisions quickly gave out and
he was reduced almost to the verse of
starvation: He stumbled on a cacne of
walrus heads, and although they had
rotted and froze he chopped them with
his knife and gladly devoured them.
Struggling onward he came to the wreck
of a schooner vrnich proved to be the
Ecarett, which was lost in November,
1900, all of her crew starving to death
except two men, one of whom died from
the effects of trfe privations he had en
dured.
Egan discovered another cache of rotten
walrus meat and also devoured this.
Then, overcome by the cold and suffer
ing, he gave up and lay down to sleep. |
The next thing he knew he was j
being aroused by natives shaking '
him and attempting to question
him. Although he was unable to
answer, the natives placed him on a
pledge and carried him to the Northwest
Cape missionary village of Gamble, where
Dr. - Lerrigo attended him. Both !
hands and both feet were frozfn, but
under Dr. Lerrigo's treatment Egan es
caped with the loss of only a couple of
f ngers and is now employed as a herder
at the Government Reindeer Station. The
cutter Bear reached the station August 2
last and there Captain Tuttle heard
Egan's story.
CELEBKATES EASTER AT
THE HOLY SEPTJXCHER
Rev. Father Caraher Vividly De
scribes Scenes at the Tomb of
the Savior.
"Easter at the Holy Sepulcher" was the
subject of a lecture delivered by Rev.
Father Terrence Caraher at St. Francis
Church last night. The lecture was a ret
rospect of a mass celebrated by the lec
turer in the chapel surrounding the sacre<l
tomb of the Holy Savior in Palestine dur
ing a sojourn in the Holy Lantt. Father
Caraher said that it was his privilege last
Easter to officiate at mass, together with
three other priests, who had been accord
ed a like honor. His descriptions of the
scenes and incidents in connection with
an event which he said was one of the
happiest in his life were vivid and inter
ested a large congregation.
"Many had sought the privilege to say
mass before our Lord's tomb," said
Father Caraher, "and 1 had the distin
guished honor of representing the Eng
lish-speaklng public at the imposing cere
mony. St. HeleÂ« was the first to locate
the tomb of Jesus Christ, and she it was
who erected the first church over the very
spot where his remains were interred.
Other tombs may surpass the holy sepul
cher in grandeur, but it far surpasses them
all in the veneration and respect in which
it is held by almost everybody on the face
of the globe. It is the most sacred spot in
all creation. It is the pivot on which the I
religious world revolves, for it is the |
fountain from which Christianity springs
forth. I was much Impressed with thi I
solemnity of the occasion when I read the j
mass, and the uppermost thought in my
mind was the wonderful resurrection of j
the Redeemer after he had lain in his
tomb for three days."

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, MONDAY, NOVEMBER 4, 1901.
ICE CLOSES
ARCTIC CAMPS
Navigation on the Yukon
Now Ended for the'
Winter.
Preparation at Dawson for
Travel Over Frozen
Trails.
PORT TOWNSBND, Wash., Nov. 3.â
The steamer Dirigo, arriving from Skag
uay this evening, brought 100 passengers
and 700 tons of canned salmon. Advices
brought by the Dirigo are to the e*ffect
that navigation is practically at an end
on the Yukon. On October 27 cake Ice
was running out of Pelly River into the
Yukon and slush ice was running at
Dawson, and the river was daily ex
pected to close.
Great preparations are being made at
Dawson and during the winter there will
be strong competition for over-ice travel.
An opposition stage line will be put on.
A large number of men are working on
roads and trails and when the river
freezes everything will be in readiness
for sledges.
The revenue cutter Rush, with Goyer
i or Brady and the Rev. Sheldon Jackson
on board, is cruising in the vicinity of
Wrangel, visiting the various Indian Vil
lages.
The Dirigo reports Southeastern Alaska
as being swept with a severe wind storm
and that she had a rough voyage down.

'TIS PRINCIPLE,
NOT POLITICS
So Says Business Agent
Mullen About th 9
Labor War.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
118 Broadway, Nov. 3.
Business Agent' J. H. Mullen of the
Building Trades Council says the contro
versy that has split the organization he
represents is not a one-man proposition
and denies that he Js the bone of conten
tion for the two factions that are waglnsj
war within the ranks of unionism. Ho
asserts that it is a principle he and his
friends are fighting for, and that they
stand between the opposing faction and
the utter destruction of the Building
Trades Council and its affiliated organiza
tions. Mr. Mullen explains as follows:
The faction of the Building Trades Council
opposed to us is claiming that all this trouble
is due to the fact that J. H. Mullen is busi
ness agent. That, I declare, is not so, an.l
I deny it because I am in a position to know
that I cut a very small figure in this contro
versy. I would like to ask these- people who
are responsible for this disrupted Council why
it is that a few months ago they made a de
liberate attempt to destroy the usefulness of
the Building Trades Council. They certainly
cannot claim that Mullen had anything to do
with that attempt lam prepared to back up
what I say with proof and coin, and I offer
to put up .*!GO against $50 that I can establish
to the satisfaction of an unbiased set of judges'
that what I s-ay is true. I will show where
they struck at the constitution of the organi
zation and defied it on two or three occasions.
The present controversy Is the result of dis
satisfaction among three of the unions with
the apportionment of delegates to the Council,
that Is, they claim it is, but it appears to
be- the old fight over again. When the San
Francisco Building Trades Council settled our
difficulties a while ago it was unanimously
agreed by all the unions that they should with
draw their old delegates and elect new. ln
stenÂ£ of choosing new delegates, however, the
unions re-eiected the old, with the result that
the fight is renewed. Now they are kicking
over the traces on this trumped-up cry of dis
proportion, and for no other reason than to
kick up a row.
Three of the unionsâ the carpenters, the paint
ers and the longshoremenâ have withdrawn
their delegates from the Council, but as I
have not been officially notified of this action
by the unions I. as business agent, have not
taken any action in the matter as yet. I don't
know what I will do until I receive the official
notification. I do know, however, that they
cannot organize another Council in this county.
Our contracts with the employers will be kept,
no matter how the controversy turns out, and
they will be fully protected.

TANGLES IN JAPANESE
BUSINESS AND SOCIETY
OAKLAND, Nov. 3.â A Japanese feud
that has been raging around Alvarado
culminated yesterday in two Japanese be
ing held to answer for trial before the
Superior Court for extortion and another
Japanese being arrested for criminal libel.
S. T&kamoto, who runs a Japanese res
taurant in Alvarado, accused K. Mlyoga
wa, who is the editor of the Japanese
Tribune in San Francisco, and M. Furu
kawa, bis clerk, of trying to extort $150
from him under threat of arrest. The
two men got $20, It is charged, which was
all the restaurant man had. The two who
were held to answer say that they were
paid the $20 for Interpreting work for the
restaurant man and they assert that they
were Interested in the moral welfare of
the Japanese communuity and knew that
the restaurant man was not keeping those
morals up to the high standard necessary
Justice' Richmond held the men to an
swer upon the showing: made by Deoutv
District Attorney Phil M. Walsh. puiy
On the way from the courtroom T. Tan
abe, another Japanese, asked anxiously
â¢Who wins?" * ' .
"You win," replied the constable, at that
moment serving a warrant upon Tanabe
for criminal libel sworn out in San Fran
cisco by the Japanese editor. Tanabe
gave an interview In a Japanese paper
Sailing the editor all kinds of hard
name*. The editor forthwith swore to a
warrant for libel.
BOILERS BLOW
UP AND WRECK
POWER HOUSE
Big Explosion in Works
of Oakland Transit
Company.
Elmhurst Is Saved From Ruin
by Fire With Great
Difficulty.
Workman Is Hurled Across the Street
a Distance of Fifty Feet, but
Escapes With Minor
Injuries.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call, !
118 Broadway, Nov 3. j
The boilers In the power-house of the j
Oakland, San Leandro and liaywards di- i
vision of the Oakland Transit Company's j
system at Elmhurst blew up to-night, |
wrecking the power-house and setting the ;
town on fire. Aid from Oakland was
sought, and had it not been for the Oak
land Fire Department and the most ener
getic work the entire town would have
been destroyed.
Buildings in the neighborhood of the
power-house were injured and wires, ma- â¢
chinery, rails, bricks and woodwork were j
blown through the walls of adjoining
buildings, the wreckage covering blocks j
around. The electric road was tied up for .
nearly three hours and people were com- i
polled to walk from San Leandro into !
Oakland in order to reach home. i
In the midst of all this only two men !
were injuced and those not severely, j
though the fireman, Michael Victor, was i
blown across the street, a distance of lifty j
feet, into another building.
The explosion occurred at 7:25 o'clock, j
and shook the surrounding country. It;
was felt in Oakland, and many believed
It was an earthquake. In San Loandro, a
mile distant, buildings were shaken in a
lively and alarming manner.
The Cause Is a Mystery.
Whatever the cause, it has done fully j
$10,000 worth of damage to the plant of ;
the electric road and a considerable
amount to buildings in the neignoorhood.
Two of the three boilers in the engine
room exploded. One of the boilers bÂ»ew
up and the concussien exploded the sec- !
ond. The explosions were practically i
simultaneous. ;
The eautern and southern walls of the j
power house were blown out, the explo
sion spending itself in that direction. Thij
wall between the engine room and the
fire room was blown out. .One of the
engines was badly damaged, but the othor
three are intact. The explosion shut off
all the power, breaking the engine and
dynamo connections and wires.
Everything was thrown into darkness,
for practically all of the light in the i
neighborhood came from the ruined pow- [
er-house. Debris was piled in all direc- j
tions, and for a while no one seemed to ;
know what had happened. Men were j
groping about in the darkness and wreck- j
age, amid fallen bricks and tangled wirea, !
some trying to reach the scene of the ex- '
plosion and some trying to get away.
Superintendent Mci.enegan of this di- j
vision happened to be in town at the ;
time and immediately took charge of !
affairs. He went right to work to get
things in some kind of shape so that the i
cars could be run. The wires were all '
down around the power-house and the i
uemolition of the power-house shut off
ail electricity, so that not only the Hay
wards line, but all of the East Oakland !
lines were stopped, they receiving power j
from the Elmhurst station. i
There were hundreds of passengers who '
had gone to Hay wards and San .Leandro j
who suddenly found themselves unable 'Â¦
to reach the city, and it was to accom- j
modate these that Mr. McLenegan bent :
his energies. In less than three hours he 'â¢
had got his wires up, had secured power |
from the Bay Counties Power Company i
and set the cars in motion.
While this was going on the people of i
Elmhurst found themselves facing an- Â¦
other danger. The Willows Hotel, across !
the street from the power-house, caught ;
fire from the hot coal that was blown j
against the wooden walls of the building.
It was this fire that necessitated a call .
for the Oakland fire department.
Fireman Hurled Across Street.
John Allanson, the engineer in charge,
was in the fireroom at the time the explo- j
sion occurred, together with Fireman I
Michael Victor and Cotflpasser Nlcolaison. j
Allanson and Nicolalson were unlnjr;ed. Â¦
Victor was blown out of the flreroonv and
across the street, fully fifty feet. Victor
was bruised and cut, but had no bones .
broken, while Billy Faught, a machinist, |
had his leg broken.
Dr. Tormey of San Leandro was sent Â¦
for and the Injured men put under his '
care. John Allanson, the engineer, says !
that he has no idea how the explosion .
occurred, that everything was in good '>
condition and ten minutes before the ex- |
plosion occurred the boilers were full of j
water. :
Superintendent McLenegan says that he !
is at a loss for an explanation, but that :
he will make a careful inspection of the '
wTecked boilers to-morrow, which may re- ;
veal something.
Gray's drug store, in which the Elm- j
hurst postofflce was also located, was
pretty badly wrecked by the explosion, :
but Courant's Hotel, on an opposite cor- '
ncr and nearer the power house, escaped !
injury. A heavy steel rail was |
blown out of the power house and overÂ«a i
car that was standing on the track and t
into the barber shop of the Willows Hotel.
The car was filled with passengers at the ;
time and had the flight of the rail b<Â»en '
shorter death and injury would have fol
lowed. __^__________

Our Growing Trade.
In 1891 the United States occupied fourth
place In the import trade of Germany
with a total of $108,528,000, being preceded
by Great Britain, with 1160,888,000: Austria-
Hungary, with $142,324,000. and Russia
with $138,040,000. In 1895 the imports Into
Germany from the United States rose to
$121,618,000. and after that year Increased
rapidly, until in 1900, when they reached
the sum of $206,750,400.
The United States thus stands far above
any other country in this trade, being fol
lowed by Great Britain, with $199,930,000"
Russia, with $173,740,000; Austria with
$172,312,000. and France, with $72.00.00?
During this period American imports Into
Germany have increased more than those
of the last-named four countries together
âNew York Press. w

IS NOT SELECTED
Coaches Still Trying Men
for Various Posi
tions.
BERKELEY, Nov. 3.â Two days' com- I
plete rest â yesterday and to-day â have
been given California's football squad.
The men were sorely in need of It, for
the weeks of practice had exhausted
them and old bruises and strains needed
care in the shape of complete rest on the
part of the players. Absence . of violent
activity has been of great benefit to the
men even after so short a time, and they
will eater upon the closing week's prac
tice refreshed in body and mind. The
team will remain in town and not spend
th>: last week away aa is sometimes done.
During the next five days the finishing
touches of the season's work will be
added. On Monday, Tuesday and Wed
nesday afternoons hard practice in the
shape of line-ups against the second
eleven will be held. Tnis will be the last
chance the coacjbe* will have to correct
faults on the Held. Besides serving as
days for final instruction, the closing
ht^avy practice will decide the line-up of
the team. On Thursday and Friday there
will be no line-up, but the men will run
through the plays and perfect themselves
in the secret signals. The work will be |
especially light on Friday â just enough
to keep the men on edge tor the struggle
with btantord the day following-.
All of the men who will represent Cali
fornia have not been definitely picked by
Coaches Simpson and Hopper. It will be
with difficulty that some of the closely
contested places are decided. Quarter is
still (.pen, wiih More and Hudson mak
ing a close run for it. At left end Dibblee,
who is recovering rapidly from his
wrenched knee, and Starr are opposed to
each other, and at right half Whipple and
Sherman. There is a possibility that
Womble will be shifted from right end to
right halt. In this case Hudson will go
to right end. leaving More at quarter, ami
Whipple and Sherman will be put en the
substitute list. Left guard Is far from
nettled. Stow'a play there has not been
satisfactory, in spite of past experience
on the varsity. Stroud, a strong, heavy
freshman, is being tried out for the piace
and will probably make it, as he is com
ing up rapidly. Duden is recovering from
numerous injuries and he will be in at
fullback to-morrow.
The men from whom the team will be
picked and the positions they will play
are as follows:

Stroud or Stow, left guard; Braly or Han
sen, right tackle; Albertson, left tackle;
Womble or Hudson, right end; Starr or

WEATHER CONDITIONS AND GENERAL
FORECAST.
The pressure continues high over tha country
west of the Rocky Mountains. There has been
a fall of about one-tenth of an Inch In pressure
along the coast from the mouth of the Colum
bia River northward. A moderate disturbance
prevails over Washington and will probably
move southward slowly. At Neah Bay 2.34
Inches are reported In the past twelve hours.
No rain has fallen In California.
Forecast made at San Francisco for thirty
hours ending midnight, November 4, 1901:
Northern Californiaâ Fair Monday, except
rain along the northern coast; cloudy at night;
continued warm; light northerly winds chang
ing to southeasterly.
Southern Californiaâ Cloudy Monday; contin
ued warm; light north wind.
Nevadaâ Cloudy Monday; continued warm;
light northerly winds.
San Francisco and vicinityâ Fair Monday;
cloudy in the afternoon and at night: continued
warm; light northeasterly winds, changing to
westerly. ALEXANDER G. McADIE.
Forecast Official.

AGED MAN MAKES
A BRAVE RESCUE
Carries Helpless Woman
From Burning House
to Safety.
Others Barely Escape From
Fire at Powell and Val
lejo Streets.
Several pÂ«ople narrowly escaped death
in a fire that destroyed a building near
the corner of Powell and Vallejo streets
early last evening. AJthougrh the hour
was early some of the occupants had re
tired and were not awakened until the
flames were almost upon them and people
had rushed in from the street. Two aged
people â a man and a woman â were almost
suffocated by the smoke, but succeeded in
making their escape.
The tire was discovered shortly after 7
o'clock in the rear of a building at 1510
Powell street. The upper portion of this
structure was occupied by C. Gianettoni,
his wife and three small children. Mrs.
Gianettoni had gone to church, leaving
her husband and the little ones in bed.
When the blaze started the man and
children were asleep and were awakened
just in time to reach the street in safety.
Another apartment of the house was oc
cupied by Bernard Schussler and wife.
Both are aged people. Henry Koster,
about 80 years of age and a veteran of the
Mexican war, was visiting the couple.
Schussler had gone across the street and
before he returned the alarm was sound
ed. Dense volumes of smoke pouring in
to the room gave the first warning to
Mrs. Schussler and Koster. The lady is
a. cripple and was unable to make her
way to the street. Koster. despite his
age, picked her up and carried her to a
place of safety. As the pair emerged
from the burning building Koster was
cheered by the spectators.
Another apartment was occupied by A.
C. Huntley and family. These people
Â¦were absent when the fire broke out. The
total loss to the building and contents will
reach $3000.
OFFER PRAYERS
FOR THE DEAD
Calvarian Society Unites
in Most Solemn
Servicea
The annual devotions of the Calvartan
Society, consisting of the Way of the
Cross, a sermon and benediction were held
yesterday afternoon at St. Mary's Cathe
dral. It Is the custom of the members of
the society to meet on the Sunday.follow
ing All Souls' <Jay and offer up prayers for
the deceased members and relatives of
members.
Rev. Father Ramm recited the Way of
the Cross, the congregation Joining in the
responses. Rev. Father Dempsey preached
the sermon, dwelling on the objects of
the society and explaining the necessity
of prayer, particularly for the souls in
purgatory- He said in part:
On Friday the Catholic church celebrated the
feast of All Paint?. On Saturday she cele
brated tbe feast of All Soule. We are as
sembled out of respect for the latter date.
Thie date brings us back to a truth In the
Aiwstles' Creed, tbe communion of saints, in
Â¦which we emphasize our belief every lime we
recite our morning prayers.
When we pray for the souls In purgatory, so
do we expect them to pray for us. We are
all of one kingdom, one great family, and
there ought to be a bond of sympathy between
us. The saints in heaven are constantly before
the throne of God, offering up prayers in our
behalf. As they were our friends on earth,
60 are they our friends In heaven.
They have rone through all the trials and
tribulations of this life, just as we are non
doing. By their prayers and petitions to the
Almighty they are constantly seeking to inter
cede for ue that we may enjoy the delights cf.
heaven as they are enjoying them. The chuich
desire* to constantly bring them before us that
â¢Â»c may profit by their example.
The day of the saint i 6 not yet passed. We
may find them in all the daily walks of life.
The man in his office, the woman in her home,
may be lurt as much a saint as those that
are even now In heaven. Doing God's will
will entitle v* to sit at the right hand of
God. and while we may not bÂ« canonised or
belong to the heroic class of salnu, we can
still attain through prayer and religious ob
servances to the came beatific state. L<et ua
then pray for the souls in purgatory that they
may be loosed from their Bine and be trans
lated from the middle state to one of perfect
bliss.
Benediction of the Blessed Sacrament
closed the exercises. During this portion
of the services the choir, under the direc
tion of Professor R. J. Harrison rendered
the following selections:
Duet. "Qui est Homo" (Roiielnl), Miss X
Black and Miss J. Sullivan; trio. "Salutarle"
mossini). Miss Black. D. Manlloyd and S. J.
Sandy: quartet. "Tantum Ergo" (Thunder)
Mies Black, Miss Sullivan, D. Manlloyd and
6. J. Sandy.

FIVE FOOTPADS CAUGHT BY POLICE
ARE ALL STRANGERS TO THE CITY
It Is Claimed That the Evidence Against Each of Them Is
Very Strong â Two Men Knocked Down and Robbed
by Four Thugs at Leavenworth and Sacramento Streets
FIVE SUSPECTED HIGHWAYMEN WHO WERE GATHERED IN BY THE POLICE IN THEIR RECORD BREAK
ING ROUND-UP OF THE FOOTPADS WHO COMMITTED THE MANY DARING HOLD-UPS THAT OCCURRED
IN THIS CITY SATURDAY NIGHT.
THE police did good work Saturday
night and Sunday morning in ar
resting five footpads, which is a
record that has seldom if ever
been equaled in police annals in
this city. Of the five men arrested two
are dangerous looking characters. These
two are Frank Nelson and Edward Da
vis, who held up Otto Fleissner and R.
N. Hamlin at the corner of Van Ness ave
nue and Jackson street about 9 o'clock
Saturday. night. They are both strangers
to the city and have been here only a few
days, taking up their quarters at 38 Minna
street. Nelson was arrested shortly after
\he robbery by Policeman Dugan, and the
bunch of keys taken by him from Hamlin
was found in his pockets when searched.
Davis is alleged to be the robber whn
presented the revolver at Hamlin' s head
while Nelson went through his pockets.
He wu arrested yesterday morning on
Minna street by Detectives Crockett and
Riordan and admitted to them that Nel
son had induced him to go out with him
Saturday night, for which he was sorry.
He had become acquainted with him in
the lodging-house. When seen in the City
Prison later he denied knowing Nelson,
and claimed he knew nothing about the
KILLED WHILE ON HIS
WAY TO ATTEND CHURCH
Aged Bernard O'Doherty Struck by an
Eastbound Mission-Street
Car:
Bernard O'Doherty. an aged deaf mute,
whose place of residence is unknown, was
killed by Mission street car No. 1152 In
front of the Grand Opera House last even-
Inir His body was taken to the morgue
and the case will be investigated by Cor
oner Leland.
O'Doherty was on his way to attend
service In St. Patrick's church and car
ried a pair of rosary beads In his hand
when he was struck. He was crossing
Mission street and did not observe the
approach of a west bound car until it was
almost upon him. O'Doherty sprang from
in front of the car on to the other track,
where an east bound car struck and killed
him.

PACKEBS'. PORTERS' AND
WAREHOUSEMEN'S OUTING
Afternoon Spent Pleasantly With
Sports and Dancing at Shell
Mound Park.
The Packers', Porters' and Warehouse
men's Union held Its first annual outing
at Shell Mound Park yesterday. The af
fair was largely attended and the guests
passed a pleasant afternoon with sports
and dancing.
Several candidates for public office were
on the grounds working zealously to fur
ther their political ambitions. Numerous
gate prizes of fancy articles and pro
visions were given away. A considerable
sum of money was realized, which will be
used for the benefit of the organization.
That the affair was so successful Is due
mainly to the efforts of the following com
mittees:

Attacked by Four Men.
Another holcl-up was reported to Cap
tain Seymour yesterday morning. H. G.
Scovern, 2416 Sacramento street; S. P.
Johnston, 304 Maple street, and R. R.
Vail, 1900 Green street, had been at a sur
prise party ac the residence of Q. Cole
man, 1126 Pine street. They left together
about midnight and Vail took a Hyde
street car to go home. Scovern and John
ston had reached about the corner of
Sacramento and Leavenworth street*
when they noticed a young man standing
close to the building on the corner anj
three other young men not far from him.
As Scovern and Johnston turned into Sac
ramento street they were struck from be
hind and knocked down. Scovern's watch,
eyeglasses and a silver lead pencil holder
Â¦were taken from him, but the robbers ha-1
dropped the watch and it was recovered
later by a conductor. Johnston lost his
hat and had his eye and forehead bruiser!.
Scovern's right knee was injured. The
robbers ran up Sacramento street toward
Jones. Scovern and Johnston describe the
young man who was standing close to the
building as 22 or 23 years of age, smooth
shaven and of fair complexion. Policeman
James J. Barry was notified of the rob
bery and is working^pn the case. It is
thought a colored woman living at 1381
Sacramento street knows two of the rob
bers.
The police are inclined to believe that
Emil Hermon, foreman in rlie Fulton Iron
Works, who was attacked by three men
at Fell and Fillmore streets shortly after
midnight while with Miss Hamilton, wlr>
was also beaten, was not the victim of
footpads, although he lost a watch chain
during the scuffle. They rather believ.<
that the attack was due to the Ironwork
ers' strike. Hermon flred a shot at hi*
assailants and the bullet passed through
the coat sleeve and grazed the skin of F.
L. Dacouillas nf 669 Guerrero street, whs
told the police he was running to Her
mon's assistance, being attracted by the
cries of the woman.
SOCIETY OF OLD FRIENDS
EATS A SOCIAL BREAKFAST
Several Candidates Partake of Re
freshment and Deliver Ad
dresses.
The Society of Old Friends gathered at
a bullshead breakfast yesterday morning
in the assembly hall of the Sunnyaide Im
provement Club at Sunny side. The heads
of half a dozen bovines and a few pecks
of beans constituted the solid part of the
feast, and a number of candidates for.
various offices contributed a flow of after
breakfast oratory.
Police Judge Conlan, as president of the
organization, delivered an address of wel
come and incidentally mentioned the fact
that Tuesday was election day ajid that
he was a candidate for re-election. Lewis
F. Bylngton. Samuel Braunhart, J. Hass
ler and B. Mahony were among the can
didates introduced to the Old Friends.
They confined their remarks to brief men
tion of their candidacy and requests for
support on election day.

HAVANA, Nor. 3.â The cigarmakers of
Havana have petitioned the Government
to expel from Havana the agents of the
Tampa Resistencia Union, who have been
trying to persuade workmen to so to
Tampa.
GREETINGS FOR
GENERAL SMITH
Big Reception Will Take
Place at Mechanics 1
Pavilion.
Programme Includes a Re
view of State Troops and
Dancing.
The general committee having in charge
the reception to General James F. Smith
met yesterday at its headquarters in tho
California Hotel and decided to hold the
affair in the Mechanics' Pavilion on the
evening of Wednesday, November 13. This
date was selected on the report of the
sub-committee, which waited on General
Smith and ascertained that it was agree
able to him.
The reception as decided upon will con
sist of an address of welcome, a review
of State troops and a dance. Music will
be furnished by the First Regiment In
fantry band. Special invitations will be
sent to Governor Gage and Mayor Phelan
as representatives respactlvely of the
State and city. It is intended to issue
7500 invitations besides 250 for the honored
guests. The fcfrm of invitation as recom
mended by the committee of which Col
onel T. E. Beck is chairman was adopted.
Carl Henry and D. Q. Troy were ap
pointed a committee to escort General
Smith to the Pavilion on the night of the
reception in conjunction with the military
escort. Colonel Huber will have charge
of the light refreshments to be served in
the upper hall. The committee will meet
next Thursday night to arrange the final
details of the reception. The following
committees will be in charge:
Entertainment â Colonel Charles Bozton,
chairman: Percy V. Long, J. J. McCarthy and
Colonel J. R. Howell.
Banquetâ Henry Lunstedt, chairman; L. F.
Walsh. F. J. Murasky.
Decoration and musicâ H. E. Curzon, chair
man; H. S. Martin, D. C. Deasy.
Printingâ J. K. Cosgrave, chairman: .T>. E.
Hayden. L>. Q. Troy.
Invitation and ticketsâ Colonel J. F. Burgin,
chairman; Colonel F. E. Beck, J. L. Gallagher.
GAMBLERS SEEK
BUCK OYE'S LIFE
Price Set on Head of
the Noted Chinese
Sport.
Buck Oye, the noted Chinese gambler,
has appealed to the police to protect him
from the wrath of highbinders. A few
days ago the hatchetmen of Chinatown
held a meeting and resolved to put Oye
out of the way. It is known that their
action was prevented by the offer of sev
eral thousand dollars* made by rival
gamblers for his head.*
The killing of the lookout for one of the
marked Chinamen's games a few morn
ings ago was the first step toward the
carrying out of the plot to slay him.
The murdered man was seated in an
. inclosure at the entrance of the gam
bling joint when two highbinders, dressed
!n dark clothes and wearing Fedora hats, |
entered the place ana fired several shots !
at him, killing him instantly. Two white
men, who are employed by the boss
gambler as bodyguards, were in the .
place at the time of the shooting. They
hurried after the murderers, but were
unable to catch them.
According to the police Oye's life Is
being sought for the reason that he is
accused of being a "wÃlcher." A short
time ago, it is claimed, two Chinese
gamblers played against his fantan game
and won over $2000, breaking the bank.
The proprietor gave them half of their
winnings and promised to pay the
balance on the following day. When they
returned to get the balance of the money
Oye repudiated their claims and ordered
them out of the den. Realizing he had
"welched," the two Chinamen hired a
Kang of highbinders to wreak vengeance
on him. A price was set on his head,
and with the hope of earning the blood 1
money the hired assassins started out to
get him. Knowing that the lookout was
armed and ready to shoot whenever it
was necessary, the murderers decided to
first put him out of the way. Two of
the best shots of the "gang" were ac
cordingly dressed in American clothes
and sent to the gambling "house to kill
him. Thinking that they were white men
the lookout allowed them to ascend the
stairs. On reaching the cage in which
their intended victim was seated they
cpened fire on the lookout, killing him in
stantly. It is believed that they Intended
to shoot Oye at the same time, but were
prevented by the appearance of thÂ° two
white men who were in the place at the
time.
